GENERAL INFORMATION 
The City of Cleveland, Ohio (the “City”) is a municipal corporation and political 
subdivision of the State of Ohio (t he “State”).  It is located in northeastern Ohio on the southern 
shore of Lake Erie and is the county seat of Cuyahoga County (the “County”).  The City was 
incorporated as a village in 1814 and became a city in 1836.   
Higher Education 
Within the County are numerous public and pr ivate two-year and four-year colleges and 
universities, including, among others, Case Western Reserve University, John Carroll University, 
Cleveland State University, Cuyahoga Community College, Baldwin Wallace University, Ursuline 
College, the Cleveland Institute of Music and the Cleveland Institute of Art. 
Financial Services 
The City is a regional financial center and is the headquarters for the Federal Reserve Bank 
of Cleveland, serving the Fourth Federal Reserv e District including Ohio, the western portion of 
Pennsylvania and eastern Kentucky and West Virginia. 
Health Care 
There are over 20 hospitals, including acute ca re and private psychiatric hospitals, in the 
County.  The Cleveland Clinic Health System, University Hospitals Health System and The 
MetroHealth System (“MetroHealth”) are all headquartered in the City. 
Transportation 
The City is a major regional center for economic and commercial activity and is served by 
diverse transportation facilities.   
Highway Transportation.  The City has immediate access to six U.S. highways and seven 
interstate highways.  The Ohio Department of Transportation (“ODOT”) is currently undertaking 
the Cleveland Innerbelt Moderniza tion Plan (the “Innerbelt Plan”) that is focused on improving 
safety, reducing congestion and tr affic delays, and modernizing in terstate travel along I-71, I-77 
and I-90 through downtown Cleveland.  The first tw o phases consisted of the demolition of the 
1959 Innerbelt Bridge, and the construction of two new eastbound and westbound I-90 bridges and 
associated improvements (known as the George V. Voinovich Bridges).  The new bridges are used 
by approximately 140,000 vehicles daily.  Over $600 million was inve sted to complete the first 
two phases.  Construction is e xpected to begin in 2025 on the next  phase of the Innerbelt Plan, 
consisting of improvements to I-90 eastbound and westbound in the Central Interchange area of 
the City and the replacement of two bridges ove r I-90.  The estimated co nstruction cost for the 
third phase is $250 million and construction is expected to span approximately 2025 to 2030.
1   
In the summer of 2022, ODOT completed cons truction of the Opportunity Corridor, a 
three-mile boulevard in the ar eas between I-490/I-77 and the City’s University Circle 
 
1 ODOT, Innerbelt Modernization Plan, as accessed on March 27, 2024. A-2 
neighborhood.  The Opportunity Corridor encompasses nearly 1,000 acres on the City’s southeast 
side and is anchored by Universi ty Circle and the Cleveland Clin ic’s main campus.  The project 
was led by ODOT and the City and included th e Federal Highway Admin istration, the Ohio 
Turnpike and Infrastructure Commission, the County, the Northeast Ohio Area Coordinating 
Agency, and the RTA as funding partners.  In add ition to transportation benefits, it is anticipated 
that the Opportunity Corridor will bring new economic devel opment and new jobs to the 
community.  The Opportunity Corridor supports an economic devel opment plan of the City and 
Greater Cleveland Partnership for the area throu gh enhanced mobility, direct access to freeways 
and the University Circle area, new frontage for potential development, improved visibility, and 
improved multi-modal access.  Approximately $257 million was invested in the Opportunity 
Corridor and related improvements.   
Airports.  Cleveland Hopkins International Airport (“Hopkins”) is the primary commercial 
service airport for north eastern Ohio.  Hopkins is situated approximately ten miles southwest of 
the downtown area and is accessible via highway from multiple directions.  Heavy rail rapid transit 
service to Hopkins is also provided from downtown by the RTA.  Opening in 1925, Hopkins is 
owned by the City and operated by the City’s Department of Port Control.  Currently, Hopkins is 
served by nine scheduled U.S. based airlines, two non-U.S. based airlines, nine regional U.S. based 
airlines, two regularly scheduled charters and four U.S. based all–cargo airlines.   
Hopkins’ approximately 935,000 square foot te rminal complex includes a multi-level 
terminal building and four concourse buildings (only three of which are in use) which support 63 
jet gates and 33 commuter aircraft  parking positions.  There are three runways at Hopkins.  
Hopkins completed a $69 milli on FAA-funded Air Traffic Contro l Tower and Terminal Radar 
Approach Control Facility in 2015, equipped with the latest technology—allowing the airport to 
seamlessly integrate with the FAA’s move to ward a Next-Generation (or “NextGen”) Air 
Transportation System.  The tower is one element in a coordinated program of airfield safety 
improvements.  In July 2016, Hopkins completed a $42 mi llion terminal mode rnization project, 
which included a new canopy over the upper roadway system to shield the departure area curb 
front from inclement weather, garage improvements, ticketing lobby renovation to include higher 
ceilings, revolving doorways, vale t staging areas, signage and other aesthetic and ancillary 
improvements at the lower roadway passenger pick-up areas.   
In 2022, the North Airfield improvement project was completed.  These improvements 
provide geometric upgrades to current FAA standards and eliminate direct aircraft access into the 
runway environment to enhance safety.  The project included replacing Taxiway A and removing 
several taxiways, which allows for easier underground utility installation and improved safety on 
the airfield by having fewer paved areas.  
In 2021, Hopkins officials announced that they were starting to prepare for a proposed $2 
billion reconstruction plan of the terminal complex and related landside infrastructure which will 
include a new terminal building and concourses,  consolidated security checkpoints, expanded 
ticketing areas and a new customs facility (the “Terminal Modernization Development Program”).  
Early-stage planning efforts regarding the Ai rport’s Terminal Mode rnization Development 
Program are underway that include the evaluati on of different developm ent alternatives for 
landside re
lated improvements.     A-3 
In December 2023, the City engaged the Paslay Group, an airport development and strategy 
consulting firm based in Fort Worth, Texas, to assist in the planning, management and 
implementation of the Terminal Modernization Development Program.  The City has received 
approximately $41.5 million in Airport Infrastructure Grants and Airport Terminal Program grants 
under the Infrastructure Investment  and Jobs Act (also known as the “Bipartisan Infrastructure 
Law”). 
Burke Lakefront Airport (“Burke”), which also is City-owned and operated, has two 
parallel runways on 480 acres adjacent to dow ntown Cleveland.  Burke recorded about 42,000 
terminal operations in 2022, the majority by air taxi operators serving business activity in the City, 
and the remainder almost entirely by corporate and private general aviation aircraft operators.  In 
2017, Burke completed the Congressionally-m andated Runway 6L/24R Safety Area 
Improvements Project.  This project lengthened the runway and brought pertinent runway-taxiway 
intersections to current FAA standards.  The project also installed an Engineered Materials 
Arresting System on the west end as the runway was lengthened to the east.   
River Transit.  The Port of Cleveland (the “Port”) is an interlake and international shipping 
center located downtown on the shores of Lake Erie and the Cuyahoga River.  The Port primarily 
handles steel and bulk commodities and is a heavy lif t port which is favorable for such items as 
automobile manufacturing equipment, presses and raw and finished steel and factory components.  
There are approximately 22,000 jobs supported by the maritime activities of  the Port, with $1.8 
billion in total personal income and local consumption. The Port averages 13 million tons of cargo 
per year. 
Railroads.  Norfolk Southern and CSX chose the C ity as their gateway to the Northeast 
and Midwest after the respective railroads restructured the rail systems following the acquisition 
of Conrail. 
Mass Transit.  The City is also served by the Greater Cleveland Regional Transit Authority 
(the “RTA”).  The RTA owns and operates a public mass transit system, providing transportation 
to a 457 square mile service area which includes 59 municipalities, one of which is the City. 
Utilities 
The City is well-served with adequate a nd reliable water and energy resources.  The 
principal source of water in the City is Lake Erie, the fourth largest of the Great Lakes and the 13th 
largest lake in the world by surface area. 2  The principal provider of potable water in the County 
is the City’s Division of Water.   
The two principal providers of electric energy in the City are Cleveland Public Power, a 
municipal utility operated by the City, and Th e Cleveland Electric Illuminating Company, a 
wholly-owned electric utility operating as a subsidiary of FirstEnergy Corp.   
 
2 National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration, Great Lakes Environmental Research Laboratory, About Our 
Great Lakes: Lake by Lake Profiles, as accessed on March 27, 2024. A-4 
Sewer services in the City are provided by the Northeast Ohio Regional Sewer District, 
which owns and operates the sewage treatment plants, and the City’s Division of Water Pollution 
Control, which owns and maintains the sewer lines within the City.   
The principal suppliers of natural gas in the County are Dominion East Ohio Gas Company, 
Columbia Gas Company of Ohio, Inc. and Shell Energy Services Co. 
Climate Initiatives 
On March 12, 2024, Mayor Bibb announced that  the City was selected by Bloomberg 
Philanthropies as one of 25 cities in the country to join Bloomberg American Sustainable Cities, a 
three-year initiative designed to  leverage federal funding to  build low-carbon, resilient and 
economically thriving communities.   Building on its effort to confront the overlapping crises of 
climate change and racial weal th inequity, the Bloomberg Phila nthropies will provide support to 
selected cities to pursue solutions in the buildings and transportation sectors through partnerships 
with PolicyLink, the Bloomberg Center for Public Innovation at Johns Hopkins University and the 
Natural Resources Defense Council.   
By participating in Bloomberg American Sustainable Cities , the City will receive a 
Bloomberg Philanthropies-funded innovation team with up to three dedicated staff with expertise 
in data analysis, insight development, human -centered design, systems thinking and project 
management to bolster the City’s capacity in driving progress on climate mitigation and promoting 
equitable outcomes.  The City will also receiv e multi-year, in-depth, customized policy and 
technical assistance in collaboration with community-based organizations to mobilize public, 
private and philanthropic investments.  
Recreation and Entertainment 
Cultural Institutions.  The City is noted for its many cultural institutions, including the 
internationally acclaimed Cleveland Orchestra a nd the Cleveland Museum of Art.  Theaters and 
entertainment centers include Playhouse Square (a theater district in th e City that includes a 
complex of eleven theaters and performance venues, currently with  seating for over 10,000), 
Public Auditorium, Karamu House and Severance Hall.  Other cultural institutions include the 
Cleveland Play House, Great Lakes Theater, Cl eveland Public Theater, Apollo’s Fire (the 
Cleveland Baroque Orchestra), Oh io Contemporary Ballet (f/k/a Verb Ballet), Da nce Cleveland, 
and the Cleveland International Film Festival.   
In September 2023, Playhouse Square completed the second phase of its development and 
restoration project which includes new theater marquees, signage for the performing arts center’s 
resident companies and technology upgrades for elements installed during the original phase of 
the transformation of Playhouse Square in 2014.  
The Rock and Roll Hall of Fame and Museum (the “Rock Hall”), a 150,000 square foot 
facility located at North Coast Harbor, opened in 1995 and has attracted approximately 13 million 
visitors to date.  The City hosted the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame inductions in 2015, 2018 and 
2021.  Future induction ceremonies will be held in the City every third year and the City is 
scheduled to host the ceremony again in October 2024.  In Oct ober 2023, the Rock Hall broke 
ground on the $135 million 50,000 square foot expansion that will include a new entry lobby and A-5 
public space, an acoustically-designed and sub-di visible performance area, a classroom and a 
1,350-person event venue.   
The Great Lakes Center for Science and T echnology (the “Science Center”), located on 
North Coast Harbor next to the Rock Hall, opene d in July 1996.  With more than 400 hands-on 
exhibits and a six-story Omnimax theater, the Science Center gives visitors the chance to explore 
science, environment and technology and their relationships to the Great Lakes.   
Other museums in the City include Museum  of Contemporary Art Cleveland, Cleveland 
Botanical Gardens, Cleveland Museum of Natu ral History, Dunham Tavern Museum and the 
Cleveland History Center.   
Recreational facilities in the City in clude portions of th e regional, 24,000-acre 
Metropolitan Park System, Cleveland Metropark s Zoo, Wade Park, Rockefeller Park, Cultural 
Gardens, Lakefront State Park a nd, outside the City, the Cuyahoga  Valley National Park.  Since 
2011, Cleveland Metroparks Zo o and Cleveland Zool ogical Society have invested $35 million 
in sizable animal habitats, incl uding African Elephant Crossing  in 2011, Rosebrough Tiger 
Passage in 2016, and Asian Highlands in 2018. The Zoo recently dedicated the Daniel Maltz 
Rhino Reserve, doubling the Zoo’s rhino habita t.  A $7.7 million renovation of the Bear Hollow 
habitat opened in August 2023.  In June 2021, the Cleveland Museum of Natural History broke 
ground on its new $46.7 million visitors center as part of its decade-long $150 million multi-phase 
projection of renovation and construction.  In  fall 2017, the Cleveland Children’s Museum 
reopened in the historic St ager-Beckwith mansion in th e Midtown neighborhood after a $10.3 
million renovation, giving the museum four times the space as its former location. 
Sports.  Professional sports are av ailable to area resi dents at various facilities located in 
the City’s downtown.  Cleveland Browns Stadium, which opened in August 1999, is an open-air 
National Football League (“NFL”) stadium located  on the lakefront in downtown Cleveland and 
is home to the Cleveland Browns.  The facility’s seating capacity  is approximately 68,000 seats.  
In addition to NFL football, the facility is suitable for major league soccer, international soccer 
and open-air concerts. 
The Gateway Sports Complex, located in the central business district of the City, includes 
Progressive Field (home of Major League Baseball’s (“ML B”) Cleveland Guardians), Rocket 
Mortgage FieldHouse (home of the National Basketball Asso ciation’s (“NBA”) Cleveland 
Cavaliers and the American Hockey League’s (“AHL”) Cleveland Monsters), and two parking 
garages, one of which is owned by the City.  The Gateway common areas consist of approximately 
13 acres and include Gateway Square, an area for outdoor entertainment and activities. 
Progressive Field, which opened in April 1994, is an open air, natural turf baseball stadium 
with a current seating capacity for approximately 36,000 people.  In January 2023, the Cleveland 
Guardians announced a multiphase renovation project to upgrade Progressive Field over the next 
two years.  Dubbed “Progressive Field Reimagined,” the first phase of  the renovation project, 
which includes a new upper deck experience and th e addition of the East 9th Street building was 
completed prior to the start of the 2024 season.  The second phase of the project, which includes 
improvements to the Dugout Club, Terrace Club, administrative offices, clubhouses and service 
level, is expected to be completed prior to th e start of the 2025 season.  Funding for the project A-6 
will be supported, in part, by the new $435 million lease agreement that was agreed upon between 
the City, the County, the State of Ohio, the Gateway Economic  Development Corporation of 
Greater Cleveland and the Cleveland Guardians.  The estimated total cost of the project is expected 
to be $202.5 million, with $135 million supported by the new lease agreement and $67.5 million 
coming from the Cleveland Guardians.  
Rocket Mortgage FieldHouse, which opened in October 1994, is a multi-functional, indoor 
facility for sporting and entert ainment events and seats appr oximately 20,000 people.  A $185 
million renovation of Rocket Mortgage FieldHouse began in September 2017 and was completed 
in September 2019.  
After hosting the 2016 Republican National Convention, the 2019 MLB All-Star Game, 
the 2021 NFL draft, the NBA’s 2022 All-Star Game, the 2024 Mid-American Conference (MAC) 
Men’s Basketball tournament, the 2024 NCAA Division I Women’s Basketball Final Four, the 
2024 Pan-American Masters Games and the 2024 Am erican Society of Association Executives 
(ASAE) Annual Meeting and Exposition, the City has established itself as a destination for major 
events. For example, the City has been selected to host the first and second rounds of the 2025 
NCAA Division I Men’s Bask etball tournament and the NCAA Division I Wrestling 
Championships in 2026.   
Hotels 
Several hotel projects are underw ay in the City, including th e renovation of historic and 
existing buildings and new constr uction.  The City’s iconic downtown Renaissance Hotel is 
currently undergoing a significan t renovation which includes reva mped guestrooms, bathrooms 
and a new lobby.  The renovations to the Renaissance Hotel are expected to be completed in 2024 
and upon completion, the Rena issance Hotel will be known as “H otel Cleveland.”  The historic 
Baker Building (circa 1919) is being converted in to a 97-room boutique property to be known as 
the “Fidelity Hotel,” with financing assistance provided by the Cleveland-Cuyahoga County Port 
Authority.  Renovations to the Fidelity Hotel are expected to be completed in 2024.  The University 
Hotel and Suites in the City’s  Midtown neighborhood, which was built in 1964 and is currently 
closed, is being converted into a Delta Hotel by Marriott.  The Erieview Tower, also built in 1964, 
will be renovated to become Ohio’s first W Hotel (the upscale brand by Marriott), supported in 
part by tax credit financing fro m the State.  The upcoming Bridge works multi-use project in the 
Ohio City neighborhood is expected to include a 16-story glass building, filled with office space, 
retail and apartments, plus a 130-room hotel. 
CITY GOVERNMENT AND ADMINISTRATIVE MATTERS 
Government 
The City operates under and is governed by th e Charter, which was first adopted by the 
voters in 1913 and has been and may be further amended by the voters from time to time.  The 
City is also subject to certain general State laws th at are applicable to all cities in the State.  In 
addition, under Article XVIII, Section 3 of the Ohio Constitution, the City may exercise all powers 
of local self-government and may exercise polic e powers to the extent not in conflict with 
applicable general State laws.  The Charter provides for a mayor-council form of government. A-7 
Legislative authority is curren tly vested in a 17-member Ci ty Council (“City Council”).  
All Council members are elected from wards.  The terms of Council members and the Mayor are 
four years.  The current terms of the Mayor and Council members expire on January 5, 2026.  The 
Council fixes compensation of City officials and employees and enacts ordinances and resolutions 
relating to City services, tax levies, appropriating and borrowing money, licensing and regulating 
businesses and trades, and other mu nicipal functions.  The presidi ng officer is the President of 
Council, who is elected by the Counc il members.  Blaine A. Griffi n was elected as President of 
Council for the term beginning January 3, 2022.  The Clerk of Council is appointed by Council. 
Pursuant to Section 25-1 of the City Charter, City Council is required to redivide the City 
into wards based on the City’s pop ulation based on the 2020 U.S. Ce nsus.  As a result of this 
requirement, City Council is required to adopt a division plan which wi ll reduce the number of 
wards to 15 by April 1, 2025 for the next regular municipal election in November 2025.   
The City’s chief executive and administrative officer is the Mayor who is elected by the 
voters for a four-year term.  The Mayor may veto any legislation passed by Council.  A veto may 
be overridden by a two-thirds vote  of all members of the Council.  On November 2, 2021, Justin 
M. Bibb was elected Mayor of the City for a te rm beginning January 3, 2022.  Prior to assuming 
office as Mayor, Mr. Bibb worked in government, business and the nonprofit sector as an executive 
and nonprofit leader, most recently as Chief Stra tegy Officer at Urbanova, a startup focused on 
improving cities.   
The Charter establishes certain administrative department s and Council may establish 
divisions thereof or additional departments.  The Mayor appoints all of the directors of the 
City’s departments. 
Employees 
As of July 2, 2024, the City had approximately 6,383 full-time em ployees, 565 part-time 
employees, and 435 seasonal employees.  Thirty-one (31) bargaining units represent 
approximately 5,084 employees (the “Bargaining Units”).   
Chapter 4117 of the Revised Code (the “Collective Bargaining Law”) establishes 
procedures for, and regulates, public employer-employee collective bargaining and labor relations 
for the City and other state and local governmental units in Ohio.  The larger units, together with 
the approximate number of employees represented by those units, include the American Federation 
of State, County and Municipal Employees, Local 100 (1,069); Cleveland Police Patrolmen’s 
Association (966); the Internati onal Association of Firefighter s (763); Municipal Foreman and 
Laborers Union, Local 860 formerly 1099 (498); Teamsters Local 507 (318); C.A.R.E./ILA, Local 
1975, AFL-CIO (261); and the Fraternal Order of Police (231).  There have been no significant 
labor disputes or work stoppages within the City in the last 35 years.    
The City has collective bargaining agreements  with each of the Bargaining Units, all of 
which formally expired on March 31, 2022.  Until a new contract is approved, the previous 
agreement remains operational; and once a new contract is approved and entered into, any changes 
in pay under the new agreement are retroactive to the expiration of the prior agreement.  The City 
has negotiated and entered into new agreements with all but four of the Bargaining Units, and these A-8 
new agreements will expire on March 31, 2025.  Negotiations with the remaining Bargaining Units 
are ongoing.  Non-union employees may receive the same wage increases as those negotiated with 
the various unions. 
City Council by ordinance establishes schedule s of salaries, wages a nd other benefits for 
City employees.  Generally, the te rms of these ordinances have been the product of negotiations 
with representatives of the employees or bargaining units, and increases in economic benefits have 
normally been provided on an annual basis. 
Police and Fire Overtime 
The City compensates the members of the City’s  police and fire depart ments at a rate of 
one and one-half times the regular rate of pay for overtime and holidays worked.  It has been the 
City’s practice that, for each one  hour unit of overtime or holiday  time worked, those employees 
receive one hour of pay at their regular rate and, at the discretion of the employee, one-half hour 
of pay at the regular rate or a credit of one-half hour of compensatory time for which they will be 
paid, to the extent that time is not used as v acation time, at their then current rate of pay upon 
termination of employment becau se of death, retirement, resigna tion, layoff or dismissal.  A 
collective bargaining agreement between the City and the Cleveland Police Patrolmen’s 
Association provides that employees may request pa yment of all or part of their compensatory 
time in money, and that if the requests exceed the total budgeted amount, the requests will be paid 
on a pro rata basis.  Similar agreements have been reached with the A ssociation of Cleveland 
Firefighters and the Fraternal Order of Police.  The total estimated amount of the City’s liability 
for accumulated compensatory time as of December 31, 2023 was $43.3 million, and that amount 
is unfunded.  That amount was ca lculated based on the employees’ then existing regular rate of 
pay.  The rate of pay used to determine any cash payments made upon retirement or other 
termination or upon the request of the employee will be the rate of pay in effect at the time of such 
determination.  The City has budgeted $6.2 million for payment in 2024 of accrued overtime and 
other separation benefits anticipated to be payable in connect ion with retirements.  The City has 
budgeted $800,000 in 2024 for deferred overtime payments requested by employees. 
Pension Obligations 
Present and retired employees of the City are covered under two statewide public employee 
retirement (including disability  retirement) systems.  The Ohio Police and Fire Pension Fund 
(“OP&FPF”) is applicable to unif ormed members of the police and fire departments.  All other 
City employees are covered by the Ohio Public Employees Retirement System (“OPERS”). 
As of July 2, 2024, approximately 5,416 City employees are covered by OPERS.  
Employees covered by OPERS cont ribute at a statutory rate of  10.0% of earnable salary or 
compensation, and the City contri butes 14.0% (actuarially establis hed for OPERS) of the same 
base.  The City’s contribution to OPERS for the year ending December 31, 2023 was 
approximately $42.0 million. 
OP&FPF covers approximately 1,967 City full-time police and fire department employees.  
Police and fire employees contribute at a statutory rate of 12.25%.  The City currently contributes A-9 
19.5% for police personnel and 24.0% for fire personnel.  The City’s contribution to OP&FPF for 
the year ending December 31, 2023 was approximately $40.1 million.    
The City’s current employer contributions to  OPERS and OP&FPF have been treated as 
current expenses and are included in the City’s operating expenditures, except to the extent that 
they are paid from the proceeds of a Police and Fire Pension levy collected by the City. 
OPERS and OP&FPF are two of five statewide public employee retirement systems created 
by and operating pursuant to Ohio law, all of which currently have unfunded actuarial accrued 
liabilities.  The General Assembly has the power to amend the format of those systems and could 
revise rates or methods of contributions to be made by the City and its employees to OPERS and/or 
OP&FPF and could also revise benefits or benefit levels.  In 2012, the General Assembly passed 
five separate pension reform bi lls intended to assist each of the five retirement systems in 
addressing its unfunded actuarial accrued liabilities.   
With the implementation of  GASB Statement No. 68, “Accounting and Financial 
Reporting for Pensions - an Amendment of GASB Statement No. 27, ” GASB Statement No. 71 , 
“Pension Transition for Contributions Made Subsequent to the Measurement Date - an  
Amendment of GASB Statement No. 68,” and GASB Statement No. 75, “Accounting and Financial 
Reporting for Postemployment Benefits Other Than Pensions,” as of December 31, 2023, the City 
had the net pension liability and the net other postemployment benefits (“OPEB”) liability reported 
and explained in the Notes to the FY 2023 Audite d Basic Financial Statem ents.  Financial and 
other information for OPERS and OP&FPF can also be found on the respective website for each 
retirement system including its Annual Comprehensive Financial Report. 
Federal law requires City employees hired after March 31, 1986 to participate in the federal 
Medicare program, which requires matching em ployer and employee contributions, each being 
1.45% of the wage base.  Otherwise, City employees who are covered by a State retirement system 
are not currently covered under the federal Social Security Act.  OPERS and OP&FPF are not now 
subject to the funding and vesting requirement s of the federal Employee Retirement Income 
Security Act of 1974.

IMPACT OF THE COVID-19 PANDEMIC 
General 
In 2020, the outbreak of COVID-19 was declared a pandemic (the “COVID-19 Pandemic”) 
by the World Health Organization (“WHO”) as well as a public health emergency at national, state, 
and local levels throughout the U. S.  The responses of government s, businesses, and individuals 
to the COVID-19 Pandemic caused widespread and significant changes in economic activity, 
impacting global, national, a nd local economic growth.  Th e U.S. Congress authorized 
approximately $6 trillion in federal assistance to individuals, businesses, and political subdivisions 
to bolster the national economic recovery.  Mitigation efforts and the development of vaccines and 
therapeutics to treat the illness resulted in an easing of the health crisis, the termination of stay-at-
home orders, and the re-opening of businesses.  In May 2023, the WHO and the U.S. ended their 
respective emergency declarations. 
Impact of the COVID-19 Pandemic on the City 
For additional information re garding the City’s management  of and recovery from the 
impact of the COVID-19 Pandemic, see the FY  2023 State Audit Repor ts available at 
www.ohioauditor.gov. 
Federal Pandemic Assistance 
General.  The U.S. Congress adopted the following legislation in order to provide federal 
assistance in managing the collateral effects of the COVID-19 Pandemic (the “Federal Pandemic 
Assistance”): 
(a) In March 2020, the Coronavirus Re lief and Economic Security (or 
“CARES”) Act, providing approxi mately $3 trillion to deliver fast and direct economic 
assistance to American workers, families and small businesses, including the establishment 
of the Coronavirus Relief Fund (“CRF”) to prov ide assistance to states  and eligible units 
of local governments; 
(b) In December 2020, the Coronavirus Response and Relief Supplemental 
Appropriations Act (“CRRSA”), providing a pproximately $900 billion in additional 
assistance; and 
(c) In March 2021, the American Resc ue Plan Act of 2021 (“ARPA”), 
providing approximately $1.9 trillion in additional assistance, including the establishment 
of the Coronavirus State and Local Fiscal  Recovery Funds (“SLFRF”).  The SLFRF 
provided $350 billion in emergency funding fo r state, local, territorial and tribal 
governments to address the rising costs of providing services and falling revenues.  
Federal Pandemic Assistance received by the City.   
CARES Act Funding.   The City was allocated approximately $60.8 million of grant 
assistance under the CARES Act and the actions of  the Ohio General Assembly.  The City used 
the CARES Act funds to reimburse itself for eligible costs incurred beginning March 1, 2020 and A-11 
ending December 31, 2020 that were related to necessary expenditures incurred due to the public 
health emergency declared with respect to th e COVID-19 Pandemic, primar ily in the areas of 
public safety and public health. 
ARPA Funding.  The City received ARPA funds in a  total amount of approximately $511 
million, the eighth largest amount awarded to a municipality in the country.  The first half of these 
funds was received in 2021, with the second half received on July 1, 2022.  ARPA funds must be 
committed by December 31, 2024 and fully expended by December 31, 2026.   
As of October 1, 2023, all of the City’s ARPA funding has been allocated.  Approximately 
$465 million  was allocated to the City’s general fund (the “General Fund” ) to offset lost 
revenues.  ARPA funding has been allocated fo r projects such as education and workforce 
investments, expansion of br oadband access, affo rdable housing deve lopment, business 
development initiatives, additional social services, an overhaul of the City's 311 call center 
services, public safety equipment and brownfield remedia tion.  In May 2023, City Council 
approved the allocation of approximately $25 milli on in ARPA funding for revitalization efforts 
on the southeast side of the City and several waterfront projects, including the design of a proposed 
North Coast Connector land bridge, the Euclid Creek Greenway trail project, park improvements 
on the west side of the City, and design work for improvements to the City’s harbor. 
Airport Assistance.  The City also received approx imately $46.3 million of CARES Act 
funds for Hopkins and $157,000 of CARES Act funds for Burke. In 2020, th e City received an 
airport safety and infrastructure grant from the FAA in the am ount of approximately $15 million 
(funded in part by the CARES Act) for the cons truction of taxiway improvements at Hopkins.   
CRRSA funds were allocated to Hopkins in the amount of $9.7 million and to Burke in the amount 
of $91,000.  The Federal Aviation Administration (“ FAA”) has awarded Airport Rescue Grants 
under ARPA in the amount of approximately $36.8 million to Hopkins and $148,000 to Burke. 
 
Water Pollution Control Assistance .  The City was awarded $1.3 million of CARES Act 
funds in 2023 to replace the sewer lines on East 97th Street.  The project will replace approximately 
1,650 linear feet of main sewer line and alleviate flooding in the area 
 
ECONOMIC AND DEMOGRAPHIC DATA 
Population 
The City is located in the Cleveland-Elyria  Metropolitan Statistical  Area (the “MSA”), 
which consists of Cuyahoga, Geauga, Lake, Lorain and Medina counties. 3  In 2020, the year in 
which the most recent Decennial Census was conducted, the MSA had a population of 2,088,251, 
ranking it the 34 th largest metropolitan area in the United States.  In 2022, the MSA had an 
estimated population of 2,079,759.

Metropolitan Statistical Area (“CMSA”), which consisted of eight northeast Ohio counties.  The U.S. Census Bureau 
ceased using the PMSA and CMSA distinctions in 2003. A-12

Survey Data, as accessed on April 1, 2024).

  A-18 
CITY DEVELOPMENT INITIATIVES 
Economic Development 
The City continues to focus its economic deve lopment strategies on identified clusters of 
regional strength including health care, health technology, paints and coatings, manufacturing, 
information technology, aerospace, and banking and finance. Retention efforts include two visitation 
programs: the Cleveland Industrial Retention In itiative for all manufacturers, distribution and 
related supply chain businesses; and a specific downtown stakeholders’ visitation program through 
Downtown Cleveland, Inc. (a development organization focused on strengthening and building the 
downtown area of the City).  Economic development highlights citywide include the Sherwin 
Williams global headquarters high rise building in the co re of downtown which is under 
construction, Cleveland Clinic’s new Global Center on Pathogen Research, part of the $500 
million Cleveland Innovation District, and the opening of the EY-Nottingham Spirk Innovation 
Hub.  
Investment in the City’s edu cational institutions continues.  Cleveland State University 
opened Washkewicz College of Engineering, its new 100,000 square foot, $60 million engineering 
building, in December 2017.  The east end of the engineering building opened for classes in spring 
2018 and the west end of the building opened fo r classes in spring 2019.  Cuyahoga Community 
College has undertaken a major construction and renovation project across all four of its campuses.  
This includes a $10 million addition and renova tion of its Advanced Technology and Training 
Center at the Metropolitan Campus which opened in October of 2019. Case Western Reserve 
University (“CWRU”) completed its CWRU think (box) program, one of the largest open-to-the-
public maker spaces in the country, in phases starting in 2015, and completed the Maltz Performing 
Arts Center in 2021. 
Growth of Cleveland’s “Eds and Meds” anchor institutions have strengthened the Health-
Tech Corridor, a hub of transit-oriented development running from Cleveland State University to 
University Circle, encompassing 1,600 acres and linking three colleges and universities and three 
major hospitals. In 2014, RTA completed a new transit station in University Circle and completed 
construction of a new transit station on Mayfield Road in August 2015. These stations complement 
RTA’s HealthLine transportation sy stem (the City’s first “bus ra pid transit” system which takes 
riders up and down Euclid Avenue) by connecting it to the City’s heavy rail system, with direct 
access to Hopkins, and providing better connectivity to the City’s second “downtown.”  The RTA 
estimates that the HealthLine has helped spur approximately $9.5 billion in development along the 
Euclid Corridor.     
The HealthLine has been vital to the growth of MidTown, which has seen $300 million of 
development since 2008, including the retention of Dealer Tire (with 550 employees in the City), 
a new Dave’s supermarket, and over 600,000 square feet of new or renovated office and lab space.  
The area also continues to prov ide a location for incubated health  technology companies such as 
Abeona Therapeutics, which now occupies ove r 30,000 square feet including a private gene 
manufacturing facility.  The Manufacturing A dvocacy and Growth Network (MAGNET), which 
helps strengthen manufacturing in northeast Ohio and grow the manufacturing sector, will convert 
a vacant school building into a workforce and innovation hub along the Corridor.  The Cleveland 
Foundation recently opened its new headquarters in MidTown and has started construction on the A-19 
MidTown Collaborative Center, a 95,000 square foot facility that will foster partnership and 
innovation with respect to econo mic development in the communi ty. Finally, Central Kitchen 
opened in a renovated facility in 2019, adding 150,000 sq uare feet of food production and co-
packing space for rapidly growing food startups such as Cleveland Kitchen (which recently raised 
$19 million of capital to expand its fermented food and kraut business).  
At the University Circle end of the Hea lth-Tech Corridor, IBM Watson Health expanded 
its headquarters at the intersection of East 105 th Street and Cedar Avenue and installed the first 
private sector, on-premises IBM quantum computer in the United States. The CWRU-Cleveland 
Clinic Health Education Campus opened in su mmer 2019 with over 477,000 square feet and at a 
cost of over $515 million.  The ne w campus houses CWRU’s School of Medicine, its School of 
Dental Medicine and its Frances Payne Bolton School of Nursing, along with The Cleveland 
Clinic’s Lerner College of Me dicine. The Cleveland Clinic br oke ground in 2022 on three new 
projects on its main campus tota ling over $1 billion of investment : (i) a new one million square 
foot Neurological Institute; (ii) a 150,000 square foot expansion of the Cole Eye Institute; and (iii) 
a 400,000 square foot expansion of the Global Cent er for Pathogen Research and Human Health.  
In addition, the Cleveland Clinic recently completed c onstruction of its $11.3 million, 22,000 
square foot BioRepository on its campus.  The BioR epository is used to store biological samples 
that will be used in research projects at Cleveland Clinic. 
The Cleveland Innovation District, a state-s ponsored collaborative partnership between 
Cleveland Clinic, University Hospitals, MetroHealth, CWRU and Cleveland State University that 
launched in January 2021, received an investment of $265 million from the State of Ohio to 
advance innovation, job creation and economic development in the City. 
Residential activity is also increasing in th e Health-Tech Corridor. The Artisan, part of a 
new development called Circle Square, recently opened and contains 298 luxury apartments built 
above several floors of parking and a floor of retail space.  The next building in the project is The 
Library Lofts which will include a new two-story Cleveland Public Library - Martin Luther King, 
Jr. branch and a 9-story building above it containing 207 loft apartments.  This complements recent 
residential developments such as One University  Circle (28-story resi dential tower opened in 
2018), Centric (7-story mixed-use  complex with 272 residential units) and the Foundry Lofts, a 
160-unit housing project in MidTown.  Along E. 105 th Street is the Fairfax Market project, a $64 
million mixed use development of 199 apartments and a 40,000 sq. ft. Meijer grocery store.  The 
Meijer grocery store opened on January 16, 2024 bringing fresh, high quality food to residents of 
the City’s Fairfax neighborhood.  Adjacent to th is is Innovation Square, an 81 unit workforce 
housing development which is under construction.  
Southwest of University Circle, the Opport unity Corridor project connects University 
Circle to I-490. The project opened up large tracts of developable sites in the heart of Cleveland’s 
east side. A focus on food research and development and manufacturing in the center of the 
Corridor led to groundbreaking on a 150,000 square foot  cold storage facility which will enable 
area businesses like Orlando Bakery to expand. Several developers are planning multi-tenant spec 
manufacturing and industrial facilities along the co rridor and the City has created a new role of 
Opportunity Corridor project manage r to coordinate site assembly, issue RFPs for key sites, and 
overall accelerate the pace of development. A-20 
In the near west side, MetroHealth has completed its multi-year campus transformation 
project.  MetroHealth opened its $86 million Critical Care Pavilion in 2016, a 1,500-space parking 
garage in November 2018, and opened the new MetroHealth Glick Center, an 11-story, 316-room 
acute-care hospital in November 2022.  The total cost for all phases of the campus transformation 
was approximately $946 million. 
The Sherwin Williams Company is constructing a new 1 million square foot headquarters 
downtown and a second research and development fa cility in the suburbs, replacing its existing 
facility at the cost of  $600 million for both fa cilities.  The headquart ers will keep over 3,100 
employees in downtown, and employ ment at the combined locations is expected to grow by 400 
in the next decade.  Construction is expected to be completed by the end of 2024. 
Major plans are underway to better connect th e City to its lakefront.  The North Coast 
Connector is a proposed land bri dge connecting the grassy lawns of Mall C and City Hall to the 
lakefront. It would span over the railroad tracks and the Shoreway to better connect downtown to 
the lakefront and the Great Lakes Science Center, Rock and Roll Hall of Fame and the Browns 
Stadium. The project would provide a safe pedest rian friendly walk from Mall C to the lakefront 
featuring pocket parks with tree canopy, patios, beach access, retail and other amenities to attract 
visitors to downtown Cleveland.  In July 2023, the first draft of the master plan for the City’s 
lakefront was publicly unveile d and included a 21.6 acre development area with 600-1,000 
residential units, 200,000-400,000 square feet of office and/or commercial space, a 200-room hotel 
rising 8-12 stories, 40,000-80,000 squa re feet of retail space and 25,000 square feet that could 
accommodate a food hall, market or entertainment space.   
The City’s recently completed Vision for th e Valley (a plan for development along the 
Cuyahoga River) shows the potential to develop the riverfront, and Bedr ock, the real estate 
company headed by the owner of the Cleveland Cavaliers, has developed a conceptual master 
development plan to develop a new riverfront neighborhood adjacent to Tower City Center, which 
would include 3.5 million square feet of c onstruction with 2,000 housing units and 1.4 million 
square feet of non-residential space.  The Cleveland City Planning Commission approved the 
master plan at its May 19, 2023 meeting. 
In January 2023, the City’s Planning Commission approved a developer’s master plan to 
move forward with the developm ent of a 316-unit residence and 400 parking spaces in Scranton 
Peninsula, the City’s largest abandoned industrial site located in the City’s Cleveland Flats area 
along the Cuyahoga River.  
 The Ohio City neighborhood continues to gr ow and be a destination for residents and 
tourists. The neighborhood is anchored by the City-owned West Side Market, the oldest 
continually operating market in the country. INTRO, a more than $125 million mixed-use project 
across from the historic West Side Market, open ed in 2022 with nearly 300 apartments, 40,000-
square feet of retail space, underground parking and a green space.  The project is one of the tallest 
mass timber buildings in the United States. Othe r apartment projects such as Church and State 
(150 units, opened in 2020) and Bridgeworks (which began construction in 2023 and consists of a 
140-unit mixed-income housing development, a 130-room boutique hotel and multiple access 
points to the lower level of the Detroit Superior  Bridge) are building on th e success of the West 
25
th Street Lofts Project (83 apartments complete d in 2016) and the Quarter (194 apartments A-21 
completed in 2018). The success of Ohio City investments has led to development along the retail 
corridor on Lorain Ave nue, with investors buying buildings from West 25 th Street to West 50 th 
Street for a variety of retail and commercial projects that include microbreweries, a home brewing 
supplier, an organic grocery store, restaurants and a shuffleboard club.  Many of the buildings are 
renovating long vacant apartments ove r first floor retail space as th e Ohio City housing market 
continues to draw new residents from all income levels looking to be in this walkable, transit-
oriented community. In 2020, Metr oHealth opened a $10 million 32,000-square foot facility 
providing outpatient and express care services to the area.  MetroHealth al so recently opened a 
new dental clinic on another site located in Ohio City.   
In Tremont, the 130-unit Electric Gardens building opened in the spring of 2021 and stage 
three of the 101-mile Ohio and Erie Canalway Towpath Trail opened in September of 2020.  
The Metroparks Red Line Greenway off-road  multipurpose trail opened in May of 2021. 
The trail connects eight densely populated neighborhoods on the west wide of the City. 
On June 5, 2023, City Council passed Ordinance No. 672-2023 which authorizes the City’s 
Director of Finance to enter into one or more contracts with various entities, including ACE Mentor 
Program of Cleveland, Cuyahoga Community College and the Urban League of Greater 
Cleveland, to implement a citywide program designed to grow lo cal workforce pipelines in key 
industries, specifically with respect to training  historically underrepres ented and marginalized 
workers.  Further, on June 2, 2023, City Council passed Ordinance No. 487-2023 which authorizes 
the City’s Director of Economic  Development to ente r into contracts with the County’s Land 
Reutilization Corporation (the “County Land Bank”) for professional servic es to implement and 
manage the City’s Site Readiness for Good Jobs Project to remediate, redevelop and acquire sites, 
including the remediation and rede velopment of City-owned sites, to attract new businesses and 
grow existing businesses with quality jobs.      
Housing and Neighborhood Development 
General.  The City continues to implement development strategies to position the City and 
its neighborhoods to capitalize on fu ture opportunities.  The City has identified areas (i) where 
significant needs must be addressed, (ii) where need and market potenti al overlap, (iii) where 
scattered site rehabilitation will be sustainable, and (iv) where the City can create new housing 
opportunities, particularly for very  low-income households.  In th ese target areas, the City has 
focused market-building and st abilization efforts, specifi cally focused on demolition, 
rehabilitation, housing renovation and land reutilization. 
Of particular importance are the efforts to  reinvest in Cleveland’s Southeast Side 
neighborhoods of Mount Pleasant, Union-Miles, and Lee-Harvard. These communities have rich 
historic legacy and deeply engaged residents who are full of pride in their neighborhoods but which 
also embody decades of disinvestment. And it is the sustained disinvestment that has left the 
Southeast Side in need of a concentrated, collaborative effort to rei nvigorate the neighborhoods. 
The City has hired staff in senior leadersh ip roles dedicated to working with nonprofit, 
philanthropic, and institutional partners to drive fo rward a plan to reinvest in the Southeast Side. 
Through this plan, the City has dedicated $15 milli on of public sector i nvestment, intended to 
leverage additional philanthropic and private sector  investment. The focus of these efforts is to A-22 
address blight and deteriorati on through targeted code enforc ement, home repair programs, 
strategic commercial corridor investments, and preparing key sites for catalytic redevelopment. 
Housing.  The City’s housing strategy embodies th e core principles of creating healthy 
mixed-income neighborhoods of choice while preserving and expanding housing opportunities for 
low- and moderate-income households.  The City has used HUD entitlement grant funds to develop 
homes and apartments for low-inco me families and for seniors, a nd to continue the City’s long-
term commitment to preserving the existing stock of HUD-assisted rental housing. 
All housing development supported by City fu nding, including tax abatement, complies 
with the City’s Green Building Standards, whic h were updated and re-issued in June 2016.  The 
standards further the City’s co mmitment to both green building and the ongoing development of 
quality housing, particularly for low- and moderate-income families.  The City’s Green Building 
Standards incorporate national standards such as LEED and Enterprise Community Partners’ 
Green Community Standards.  
In 2022, the City adopted a new residential tax abatement program to support continued 
residential development throughou t the City. The new program pr ovides more generous tax 
abatement to areas of the City that have traditionally seen less market rate development. The new 
tax abatement program is also designed to bring revenue into the City to help financially support 
general funded programs and services throughout the City. The new tax abatement program took 
full effect January 24, 2024 and will provide predictable incentives through 2030. 
Downtown Cleveland, Inc. reported in its 2023 Midyear Market Update that apartment 
occupancy in the City has risen to over 91 %, with over 1,700 apar tments, townhomes, and 
condominiums under construction.  Large scale housing projects recently completed include:  
 The Edison, a 306-unit new construction building providing market rate housing;  
 The Mueller Building, a renovation project creating 51 units as of 2019;  
 Centric, a new construction project creating 269 units;  
 11702 Euclid, a new construction project creating 53 units;  
 The Quarter, a new construction project creating 194 units;  
 the Halle Building, a renovation project creating 122 units;  
 The Athlon, a renovation project converting th e Cleveland Athletic Club to offer 171 
luxury units;  
 The Beacon, a new construction project creating 187 market rate units;  
 Church + State, a 158-unit Ohio City building;  
 The May, with over 300 units in a historic former department store; 
 The Terminal Tower Residences, a nearly 300 unit complex in th e iconic Terminal 
Tower Building; 
 55 Public Square, a renovation project that refur
bished the commercial office space on 
the upper floors and converted the lower floors to residential units;  
 Euclid Grant, an $80 million project wh ich opened in early 2021 and includes 240 
apartments, 20,000 square feet of retail space, and 200 parking spaces downtown;  A-23 
 The Lumen at Playhouse Square, a $135 mil lion apartment complex featuring a 34-
story building with more than 300 units, which was completed by the Playhouse Square 
Foundation in October of 2020;  
 75 Public Square, the historic commercial building which was converted to a 114-unit 
residential building; and 
 The Roost Apartment Hotel, a boutique hotel with apartment-style living on three floors 
and 62 units, which opened in March 2022. 
Other housing construction and renovation is underway in the City: 
 Development is underway for the City Cl ub Apartments, a new 23-story mixed use 
building at 776 Euclid Avenue that will include 304 apartments and penthouses;  
 Plans are currently underway to revitalize Erieview Tower and Galleria along E. 9th 
Street into a mixed-use de velopment that will include  227 apartments, a 210–bed 
luxury hotel and improvements to 300,000 sq. ft. of office space.  The total 
development including a redesign of the existing shopping center into a lifestyle center 
is estimated at $217 million;  
 Construction has commenced on a seven-st ory development at 1060 Bolivar Road, to 
be known as Ten60, which will include 184 downtown apartment units. 
 
The City has also partnered with experienced  non-profit housing de velopers and service 
providers to create permanent supportive housing units for chro nically homeless persons with 
disabilities.  The City allocat ed $30 million of American Rescue Plan Act (“ARPA”) funds for 
market rate and affordable gap financing for housing development. The Department of Community 
Development issued a Request for Proposals in winter of 2023 and received 92 submissions 
representing over 5,000 housing un its of proposed development w ith over $1.3 billion in total 
development costs. 
In addition, Habitat for Humanity has continued to expand its affordable home ownership 
model using volunteer labor and homebuyer sweat equity, selling over 60 homes to low-income 
families from 2016 to 2022.  The City recently provided $5 million in funding to Habitat for 
Humanity for development of additional affordable housing units.  In 2022 the following housing 
projects were completed: Commodore Place Apar tments (198 unit apartment building), Circle 
North Homes (30 single-family homes), St. Jo seph Commons (68 units of permanent supportive 
housing), EDEN V (11 housing units), and Colfax Homes (36 single-family homes). 
Neighborhood Transformation Initiative   
General.  In 2017, the City introduced various new programs as part of the Neighborhood 
Transformation Initiative (“NTI”), a multi-fa ceted approach to growing, revitalizing, and 
sustaining local communities within the City that  have traditionally not seen investment. These 
programs include investments in economic development pr ojects, afford able housing 
rehabilitation, small business crea tion, and revitalization efforts that reduce blight and improve 
and preserve critical structures in neighborhood communities within the City. Additionally, these 
programs are envisioned to leverage privat e investments, stabilize market values, and create wealth 
and opportunity for City reside nts.  Moreover, the programs  include a $44 mil lion mixed-use 
development fund and down payment assistance fo r homeowners to help leverage high loan-to-A-24 
value mortgages and stabilize single-family ho meowner and rehab markets. Additionally, it 
incorporates a home repair program that considerably enriches living conditions and home values 
for existing residents and a reta il-focused business incubator program that supports and boosts 
entrepreneurs by providing them with critical resources to star t innovative and sustainable new 
businesses that spur economic growth in under-served communities within the City.  Further, the 
City’s Down Payment Assistance Program helps to ensure that homeownership is accessible to 
potential buyers by providing the cash needed to satisfy traditional mortgage loan program 
requirements. The Down Payment Assistance is secured by a second mortgage on the home, but 
is fully forgiven after the buyer remains in the home for five years if certain criteria are met. 
To date, over $10.5 million in City/NTI funds have been invested in these neighborhoods, 
leading to the following: 
 One new-construction mixed-use development; 
 GlenVillage – retail incubator comprised of seven small businesses;  
 Environmental remediation and redevelopment of land into new public green space; 
 Streetscape and infrastructure improvements along E. 105th Street; 
 Five completely rehabilitated homes; 
 52 homes repaired with another 30 scheduled for repair services; and 
 Over 30 residents receiving mortgage assistance through mort gage loans, down 
payment assistance and/or homebuyer education. 
 
These critical investments by the City have attracte d over $40 million in private 
investment. 
Glenville Neighborhood Projects.  The NTI has undertaken several projects in the Glenville 
neighborhood including: 
 Glenville CircleNorth, the first mixed-use project developed through the NTI.  The $16 
million development consists of 63 one- and two-bedroom apartment units and 13,000 
square feet of first floor commercial space. Twenty percent of the apartment units are 
reserved for those individuals whose income is at or below 80% area median income.  
 GlenVillage, a business incubator locate d within the Glenville CircleNorth 
development. GlenVillage provides a physical location to aspiring entrepreneurs to test 
their business ideas and build their entrep reneurial acumen. Loca l partners provide 
training and mentorship during the incuba tion period to help overcome the initial 
hurdles. GlenVillage currently has seven minority-owned businesses located in the 
space. There are four food-based businesse s, including one coffee/bakery shop, one 
health food bistro and a seafood restaurant. GlenVillage is also home to a barber shop 
and two clothing businesses.  
 A City partnership with Community Housing Solutions to provide repair services to 
seniors in the target areas of Glenville and Buckeye-Woo dhill that are enrolled in 
Homestead Tax Exemption program. Repair services include health and safety needs, 
as well as cosmetic repairs that will improve quality of life.  A-25 
 New construction on vacant parcels and rehabilitation of existing structures, providing 
both market rate and affordab le housing options th at improve market confidence and 
restore housing values. Diversifying housing stock creates housing options for new and 
existing residents at all income levels, creating true mixed-income communities. The 
City is working with different partners to develop at least 26 new single family homes 
on City-owned infill lots in the Glenville neighborhood to be sold at market rate and to 
rehabilitate 11 vacant homes in the Glenville neighborhood.   
Clark-Fulton Neighborhood.  In the Clark-Fulton neighborhood, plans are underway to 
develop a cultural and economic hub for the Latino community centered on development of micro-
retail spaces for small businesses and a specialty grocer. 
Demolition.  The City continues its aggressive efforts to demolish vacant, abandoned, and 
deteriorated properties. In 2022, the City allocated $15 million of its ARPA funds for demolition 
and secured a $4 million in demolition funding from the Ohio Department of Development 
(“ODOD”). To inform a data driv en approach to demolition and code enfo rcement, the City 
conducted an assessment of every parcel within its borders id entify all structures needing 
demolition or other intervention. In 2023, the C ity submitted another application to ODOD 
requesting an additional $10 million in grant funding to raze 646 targeted structures.  In 2023, the 
City razed 556 condemned structures. Between 2006 and 2023, the City razed a total of 13,283 
condemned structures. 
These demolitions have posit ioned the City for redevel opment by eliminating nuisance 
properties and strategically assembling land.  They have helped stabilize the real estate market and 
increase the availability of developable sites,  thereby supporting many of the new construction 
projects undertaken in recent years.   
Today, the City uses data and strategy to focus its priority on demolishing the most harmful 
problem properties The City is also taking steps to save properties from demolition by preventing 
their decline and promoting their maintenance and reha bilitation. This multi-o rganization effort 
draws on the work of national leaders in housing policy and code enforcement, with the goal of 
protecting neighborhoods from illegal activity, addressing funding gaps that inhibit rehabilitation, 
and improving the City’s code enforcement system s, thereby limiting the need for demolition.  
Other efforts that the City takes for targeted demolitions is to collaborate with other departments, 
the SE Strategy Team and third-party partners and developers who are operating rehabilitation and 
new construction projects.   Our collaborative efforts ensure that demolished structures are 
meaningful when redeveloping the community or  are pulled from demolition efforts for targeted 
rehabilitation. 
 
Residents First.   Early in 2024, City Council passed Residents First, a comprehensive 
legislative package around housi ng issues proposed and developed by City Administration in 
collaboration with City Council, community groups, and housing experts. The purpose behind the 
expansive legislation is to protect the City’s housing stock today and into the future. 
 
Building & Housing (“B&H”) is no longer taking a one-size f its all approach to code 
enforcement. Property owners now must have a local agent if they themselves live outside the 
Cleveland region. Rental properties must be registered with the City as before, but now must also A-26 
be current on property taxes and utilities, free fr om violations, have a lead safe certificate, and 
have the heating system certified if greater than three units in order to get a rental occupancy 
certificate. Vacant properties must be registered with a local point of contact and one to three 
family vacant structures must ha ve an exterior inspection prior to  transfer. Parking garages must 
be registered and inspected. In addition to the potential for cri minal prosecution of housing code 
violations, housing inspectors may now also issue civil tickets for housing code violations, which 
operate similarly to a parking ti cket or similar infraction. A ll of these changes have been 
implemented in order to preserve the City’s housi ng stock, and in particular  its robust inventory 
of naturally occurring affordable housing. 
 
Paired with the passage of this legislati on was a reorganization of B&H. Prior to the 
reorganization, inspectors worked on both perm its and code enforcement complaints. The 
reorganization has split staff into permit inspectors and code enforcement inspectors. This allows 
staff to specialize and be more responsive to thei r customer, whether that be a contractor with a 
permit or a citizen considered about the house down the street that is in disrepair. 
 
Strategic Code Enforcement.   The passage of Residents First and the department 
reorganization has enabled B&H to  pivot to strategic code enfo rcement. Historically, B&H has 
been reactive, largely engaging in code enforcement activity on a complaint only basis. By moving 
to a proactive approach, B&H will now catch property maintenance issues early on, which 
positions the city to more easily work with homeowners, landlords, and other investors to maintain 
their properties. Inspectors will rotate thr ough neighborhoods to survey properties and address 
issues, focus on commercial corridors, and inspect re ntal properties at the start of this transition. 
Strategic code enforcement is a national best practice and will provide and sustain safe, affordable 
housing for City residents 
 
Rehabilitation of Historic Buildings.  The City also continues to focus on the rehabilitation 
of historic buildings in the City.  On March 24, 2023, Council approved legislation for the City to 
purchase the 250,000 square foot ArtCraft building located on Superior Avenue and provide for a 
design-build renovation of the building with Tu rnDev, a developer and current owner of the 
ArtCraft building.  The vacant historic building will be renovated for use as the City’s new police 
department headquarters.  TurnDev agreed to do a total renovation of the building at a cost not to 
exceed $90 million which will include plumbing, electrical, HVAC improvements and a new roof 
and elevators at the building.  The renovation will also include building a new parking garage and 
surface parking, as well as the furniture, fixtur es, equipment, information technology business 
systems and other related expenditures.  In addi tion to the new police headquarters, the ArtCraft 
building will also house the Police Museum.  The renovation of the ArtCraft building is expected 
to be completed in early 2025.  Upon completion, TurnDev will transfer ownership of the building 
to the City.  
Brownfield Redevelopment  
The City is committed to the elimination of vacant and condemned houses and commercial 
structures and has identified seven priority ar eas for potential economic  growth.  Hundreds of 
structures have been demolished in these areas to  clear and assemble sites for redevelopment.  
These priority areas were selected because of their proximity to highways and existing 
infrastructure and the opportunity for economic growth.  The City ’s brownfield redevelopment A-27 
efforts, in partnership with the County, have resulted in the assessment of over 1,150 acres at over 
180 sites throughout the City. 
On June 17, 2022, as part of the Ohio Brow nfield Remediation Program, the Ohio 
Department of Development awarded $192 million to fund 112 Brownfield Remediation projects 
throughout Ohio. These grants included nine Br ownfield Assessment Grants and 16 Brownfield 
Clean Grants in the County totaling over $50 million.

DEFINITIONS AND SUMMARY OF CERTAIN TERMS 
AND PROVISIONS OF THE TRUST AGREEMENT [THIS PAGE INTENTIONALLY LEFT BLANK] DEFINITIONS AND SUMMARY OF CERTAIN TERMS AND 
PROVISIONS OF THE TRUST AGREEMENT 
When used in this Official Statement, the following terms have the meanings set forth below; 
provided that the definitions set forth below are qualified in their entirety by reference to the Trust 
Agreement, as defined below, which is available from the Trustee. 
“Additional Bonds” means the Additional Bonds aut horized to be issued pursuant to the Trust 
Agreement, irrespective of whether such Additional Bonds are designated bonds or notes. 
“Additional Bonds Test” means the requirements fo r the issuance of Additional Bonds described 
below in paragraph (ii) under “Authorization of the Issuance of Bonds.” 
“Adjusted Net Revenues” means, for any period, Net Revenues Available for Debt Service adjusted 
to reflect increases or decreases resulting from the following: 
(a) Rates in effect at the time of issuance of the proposed Series of Additional Bonds; 
(b) Revenues anticipated to be generate d based upon rates established (and to be 
placed in effect within two year s from the date of issuance of the proposed Series of Additional 
Bonds) in a rate ordinance adopted by the Council prior to the issu ance of the proposed Series of 
Additional Bonds; 
(c) Additional Net Revenues anticipated to  result from any addition, extension or 
improvement to the System that the City has constr ucted and that is in operation on the date of 
issuance of the proposed Series of Additional Bonds, but that was not in operation during the entire 
period; 
(d) Additional Net Revenues anticipated to result from the operation of existing 
facilities or existing collection systems that the C ity will acquire or commence operating with the 
proceeds of the proposed Series of Additional Bonds; and 
(e) Changes in Operating Expenses antic ipated to result from any additions, 
extensions and improvements to the System to be financed with the proposed Series of Additional 
Bonds. 
“Appreciated Principal Amount” means, as of any date of computation with respect to any Capital 
Appreciation Bond, an amount equal to  the principal amount thereof at its  initial offering plus an amount 
of interest that, based on semiannual compounding on each Compounding Date from the date of delivery 
on the basis of a 360-day year of twelve 30-day mont hs, will produce a yield approximately equal to the 
yield to maturity for such Bond specified in or pursu ant to the applicable Supplemental Bond Ordinance.  
A schedule setting forth the Appreciated Principal Amount of Capital Appreciation Bonds as of the 
Compounding Dates may be set forth in the form of such Capital Appreciation Bond.  The Appreciated 
Principal Amount with respect to any date other than the Compounding Dates is the Appreciated Principal 
Amount on the next preceding Compounding Date or date d date as noted on the fo rm thereof, as the case 
may be, plus the fraction of the difference betwee n the Appreciated Principal Amount on such next 
preceding Compounding Date or dated date and the ne xt succeeding Compounding Date that equals the 
ratio of (a) the number of days from such next preced ing Compounding Date or date d date to the date for 
which the determination is being calculated to (b) the total number of days from such next preceding 
Compounding Date or dated date to the next succeeding Compounding Date. 
B-1“Assumed Amortization Period” means the period of  time specified in paragraph (a) or paragraph 
(b) below, as selected by the Director of Finance: 
(a) Five years; or 
(b) The period of time, exceeding five years, set forth in a written opinion delivered 
to the City, of a Financial Expert, as being not longer than the maximum period of time over which 
indebtedness having comparable terms and security issued or incurred by similar issuers of 
comparable credit standing would, if then bein g offered, be marketable on reasonable and 
customary terms. 
“Assumed Interest Rate” means the rate per annum (determined as of the last day of the calendar 
month next preceding the month in wh ich the determination of Assumed In terest Rate is being made) set 
forth in an opinion delivered to the City of a Financ ial Expert, as being not lower than the lowest rate of 
interest at which indebtedness having comparable terms, security and federal tax status amortized on a level 
debt service basis over a period of time equal to the Assumed Amortization Period, and issued or incurred 
by similar issuers of comparable credit standing would, if being offered as of such last day of the calendar 
month, be marketable on reasonable and customary terms; provided that such rate may not be higher than 
the highest rate, if any, permitted by law at which such Bonds could be sold on that day. 
“Balance Subfund” means the Subfund of that name established pursuant to the Trust Agreement. 
“Balloon Bonds” means any Bonds or Bond Anticipation Notes (a) 25% or more of the principal 
payments of which are due in a single year, excludi ng any such principal payments that are subject to 
mandatory sinking fund requirements in a prior year, or (b) 25% or more of the principal of which may, at 
the option of the holder or holders thereof, be redeemed at one time. 
“Bond Anticipation Notes” means any notes issued in  anticipation of the issuance of a Series of 
Additional Bonds. 
“Bond Counsel” means any law firm appointed by the City with nationally recognized standing in 
the field of municipal law whose approving opinio ns are generally accepted by purchasers of municipal 
bonds. 
“Bondholder” or “Holder” or “Owner” or words of similar import mean, when used with reference 
to any Bond, any Person in whose name the Bond is registered on the Register, or his duly authorized 
attorney in fact, representative or assigns. 
“Bond Registrar” means the Trustee acting in the capacity of Bond Registrar under the Trust 
Agreement, unless and until a successor Bond Registrar becomes such pursuant to applicable provisions of 
the Trust Agreement, each of which must be a transfer agent registered in accordance with Section 17(A)(C) 
of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934. 
“Bond Service Charges” means the principal of and the interest and any premium on the Bonds. 
“Bonds” means, collectively, the Series 2016 B onds, the Series 2024 Bonds and any Additional 
Bonds. 
“Bond Year” means, as to any Series of Bonds, each one-year period (or shorter period from the 
date of issue of the Bonds of that Series) that ends on the close of business on the last day of a compounding 
interval used in computing the yield on the Bonds of that Series for purposes of the Code. 
B-2“Book-Entry Form” or “Book-Entry System” means a form or system in or under which (i) the

issued by the City only to a Depository or its nomi nee as registered owner, with the bonds “immobilized” 
in the custody of the Depository, and the book entry maintained by a person or persons other than the City 
or the Trustee being the record that identifies the owners of beneficial interests in the Bonds of such Series 
and the Bond Service Charges on the Bonds of such Series. 
“Capital Appreciation Bonds” means Bonds, the interest on which, during their entire term or any 
portion thereof, is payable only as a component of the Appreciated Principal Amount at maturity or 
redemption pursuant to the applicable Supplemental Bond Ordinance, and includes Compound Interest and 
Income Bonds.

required to have a perfected, first-priority security interest in the securities;

of its affiliates, or bank whose holding parent comp any is rated in the top two short-term or long-
term rating categories by at least two Rating Agencies; 
(i) Investment agreements the issuer of which is a Qualified Financial Institution; 
(j) Any debt or fixed income security, the issuer of which is rated in the highest rating 
category by at least two Rating Agencies; 
(k) Star Ohio, the Ohio Subdivision’s Investment Fund created and administered by 
the Treasurer of the State pursuant to Section 135.45 of the Ohio Revised Code; 
(l) demand deposits, including interest-b earing money market accounts, trust 
deposits, time deposits or bankers acceptances (in each case having maturities of not more than 360 
days) of a domestic bank (including the Trustee and any bank affiliated with the Trustee), including 
a branch office of a foreign bank, which branch office is located in the United States, provided that 
such bank at the time of purchase, has a short-term bank deposit rating of “Prime-1” or better by 
Moody's and a rating of “A-1” or better by Standard & Poor's; and 
(m) money market mutual funds, including,  without limitation any mutual fund for 
which the Trustee or an affiliate of the Trust ee serves as investment manager, administrator, 
shareholder servicing agent, and/or custodian or subcustodian, notwithstanding that (a) the Trustee 
or an affiliate of the Trustee receives fees from such funds for services rendered, (b) the Trustee 
charges and collects fees for services rendered pursu ant to the Trust Agreement, which fees are 
separate from the fees received fro m such funds, and (c) services performed for such funds and 
pursuant to the Trust Agreement may at times duplicate those provided to such funds by the Trustee 
or its affiliates. 
Any Supplemental Trust Agreement may provide th at any obligations (other than Government 
Obligations or Government Certificates) otherwise constituting Eligible Investments will not constitute 
Eligible Investments for all or any portion of the mone ys on deposit in any one or  more Special Funds if 
the officer or officers of the City  executing any Supplemental Trust Agreement on behalf of the City 
determine that such provision is necessary in order to enhance the perceived creditworthiness of the Bonds 
or any Series of Bonds (which determination will be  conclusively evidenced by the execution by such 
official or officials of any Supplemental Trust Agreement containing such provision).  Any investment that 
would, at the time of such investment, qualify as a lawful investment for interim moneys under 
Section 135.14 of the Ohio Revised Code (or any statute that supersedes that section) for any governmental 
body subject to its restrictions, will also qualify as an Eligible Investment. 
“Event of Default” means any event of default under the Trust Agreement.

of the City, from time to time, pursuant to the Trust Agreement, each of which must be a municipal advisor 
registered with the Securities and Exchange Commission and the Municipal Securities Rulemaking Board 
under the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, as amended.
