as set forth in Section 5(a). 
“State” means the State of Ohio. 
D-2“Trust Agreement” means the Master Trus t Agreement, effec tive as of April 14, 
2016, as supplemental and amended from time to time, by and between the City and Zions 
Bank, a Division of ZB, National Association, as trustee. 
SECTION 3. Provision of Annual Information. 
(a) The City shall provide (or cause to be provided) not later than the Filing Date to the 
MSRB an Annual Filing, which is c onsistent with the requiremen ts of Section 4.  The Annual 
Filing shall be submitted in an electronic format and contain such  identifying information as is 
prescribed by the MSRB, and may be submitted as  a single document or as separate documents 
comprising a package, and may cr oss-reference other informati on as provided in Section 4; 
provided that if the Audited Financial Statements  of the Division are unavailable on the Filing 
Date, the City shall submit unaudited financial statements, if available, with the Annual Filing on 
the Filing Date and then subm it the Audited Financial Statements of the Division when 
available.  If the Division’s Fiscal Year changes,  it shall give notice of such change in the same 
manner as for a Specified Event under Section 5. 
(b) If the City is unable to provide to the MSRB an Annual Filing by the Filing Date, 
the City shall, in a timely manner, send a notice to the MSRB in an electronic format as prescribed 
by the MSRB. 
SECTION 4. Content of Annual Filing.  The Division’s Annual Filing shall contain 
or include by reference the following:

Holders of at least a majority of the principal amount of the Series 2024 Bonds  then outstanding.

the Annual Filing for the year in which the ch ange is made should present a comparison (in 
narrative form and also, if feasible, in quantitative form) between the financial statements or 
information as prepared on the ba sis of the new accounting princi ples and those prepared on the 
basis of the former accounting principles.

the Holders and Beneficial Owner s from time to time of the Series 2024 Bonds.  The exclusive 
remedy for any breach of the Certificate by the City  shall be limited, to the extent permitted by 
law, to a right of Holders and Beneficial Owners to institute and maintain, or to cause to be 
instituted and maintained, such proceedings as may be authorized at law or in equity to obtain the 
specific performance by the City of its obligations  under this Certificate in a court in Cuyahoga 
County, Ohio.  Any such proceedings shall be ins tituted and maintained only in accordance with 
Section 133.25(B)(4)(b) or (C)(1) of the Revised Code (or any li ke or comparable successor 
provisions); provided that  any Holder or Beneficial Owner may exercise indi vidually any such 
right to require the City to specifically perform its obligation to provide or cause to be provided a 
pertinent filing if such a filing is due and has not been made.  Any Beneficial Owner seeking to 
require the City to comply with this Certificate shall first provide  at least 30 days’ prior written 
notice to the City of the City’s  failure, giving reasonable detail of such failure, following which 
notice the City shall have 30 days to comply.  A default under this Certificate shall not be deemed 
D-6an event of defaul t under the Trust Agr eement, and the sole remedy und er this Certificate in the 
event of any failure of the City to comply with this Certificate shall be an action to compel 
performance.  No person or entity shall be en titled to recover mone tary damages under this 
Certificate.

this Certificate shall be subject to the availability of funds and their annual appropriation to meet 
costs that the City would be required to incur to perform those obligations.  The City shall provide 
notice to the MSRB in the same manner as for a Specified Event under Section 5 of the failure to 
appropriate funds to meet costs to perform the obligations under this Certificate. 
SECTION 10. Termination.  The obligations of the City  under the Certificate shall 
remain in effect only for such period that the Series 2024 Bonds are outstanding in accordance 
with their terms and the City re mains an Obligated Person with respect to the Series 2024 Bonds 
within the meaning of the Rule.  The obligation of the City to provide the information and notices 
of the events described above sh all terminate, if and when the City no longer remains such an 
Obligated Person.  If any person, other than the City, becomes an Obligated Person relating to the 
Series 2024 Bonds, the City shall us e its best efforts to require su ch Obligated Person to comply 
with all provisions of the Rule applicable to such Obligated Person. 
SECTION 11. Dissemination Agent.  The City may, from time to time, appoint or 
engage a dissemination agent to assist it in carry ing out its obligations under this Certificate, and 
may discharge any such agent, with or without appointing a successor dissemination agent. 
SECTION 12. Beneficiaries.  This Certificate shall inure solely to the benefit of the 
City, any dissemination agent, th e Participating Underwriter and Holders and Beneficial Owners 
from time to time of the Series 2024 Bonds, and shall create no rights in any other person or entity. 
SECTION 13. Recordkeeping.  The City shall maintain records of all Annual Filings 
and notices of Specified Events and other events  including the content of  such disclosure, the 
names of the entities with whom such disclosures were filed and the date of filing such disclosure.

1 INTRODUCTION AND PURPOSE 
1.1 Introduction and Purpose 
GPD Group, Inc. (GPD) and Stantec have been retained by the City of Cleveland, Ohio (City) 
Department of Finance and the Division of Water Pollution Control (WPC) to provide engineering (GPD) 
and financial (Stantec) consulting services to prepare a Bonds Feasibility Report (Report) for the planned 
issuance of Series 2024 Water Pollution Control Revenue Bonds (Bonds or Series 2024 Bonds). The 
proceeds of the Bonds will be used to fund capital improvements to WPC’s wastewater collection system 
(System), fund a debt service reserve requirement, and pay costs of issuance of the Bonds. WPC’s 
System includes sanitary sewers, combined sanitary and storm sewers, separated storm sewers, 
wastewater pump stations and related appurtenances and equipment to collect and transfer wastewater 
and stormwater flow. The System does not include the regulator weirs (structures on sewer systems that 
permit the discharge of excess flow into a relief sewer and the design dry weather flow to continue up to 
its intended outlet) or wastewater treatment facilities. All wastewater collected by the System is conveyed 
to the Northeast Ohio Regional Sewer District (NEORSD) facilities for further transportation and 
treatment. 
The Report includes: 
• A description and condition summary of the System. 
• An overview of WPC's organization and management. 
• A summary of the status of current and proposed capital projects and improvements. 
• A review of regulatory compliance. 
• A summary of historical and projected customer statistics. 
• A review of current and projected financial information. 
• Conclusions regarding the technical, organizational and financial aspects of WPC. 
The technical and financial data reviewed in preparing this Report include existing records and 
documents provided by WPC and the Division of Fiscal Control. The Report summarizes the work 
completed up to the date of the issuance of the Report. Changed conditions occurring or becoming 
known after such date could affect the material presented and the conclusions reached herein to the 
extent of such changes. 
GPD and Stantec have not independently verified the accuracy of the information provided by WPC and 
others. However, our Report is based upon the belief that such sources have been performed with the 
highest standard of care and as such are complete, reliable and the information obtained therefrom is 
appropriate for the analysis undertaken and the conclusions reached herein. To the extent that the 
information provided to GPD and Stantec by WPC, Fiscal Control and others is not accurate or not 
complete to all relevant details, the conclusions and recommendations contained in this Report may  be 
incorrect, vary, and are subject to change. In addition, the scope of GPD’s and Stantec’s review did not 
include any pending or threatened litigation that may impact WPC. The City has stated that there is no 
significant litigation that it believes would have any material impact on WPC’s operations, and as such, 
GPD and Stantec have prepared its Report accordingly and under the presumption of the accuracy of 
such statement. 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 1-2 
 
 
 
In completing this Report for WPC, GPD and Stantec are not serving in the role of a “municipal advisor” 
under the regulations of the Securities and Exchange Commission. As such, GPD and Stantec 
individually is not: (a) recommending any action regarding municipal financial products or the issuance of 
municipal securities; and (b) is not acting as a registered municipal advisor to the City, and does not owe 
a fiduciary duty to the City pursuant to Section 15B of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934, as amended 
by the Dodd-Frank Wall Street Reform and Consumer Protection Act, with respect to the information and 
material prepared in connection with this Report. The City should discuss the information and material 
prepared in connection with this Report with any and all internal and external financial and other advisors 
whom the City deems appropriate to provide such guidance before acting on this information and 
material. 
Accordingly, since GPD and Stantec individually is not a municipal advisor registered with the Securities 
and Exchange Commission, the City acknowledges that: (a) it has retained the services of an 
independent registered municipal advisor, who, during the past two (2) years, was not associated with 
GPD or Stantec, and that (b) GPD has complied with the requirements set forth in the federal Securities 
Exchange Act, Municipal Advisor Rule (17 CFR 200, 240, 249), which requires that the engineering 
company: (i) receive from the municipal entity a representation in writing that it is represented by, and will 
rely on the advice of, an independent registered municipal advisor; (ii) provide written disclosure to the 
municipal entity that GPD is not serving as a municipal advisor and, with respect to the municipal entity, is 
not subject to the statutory fiduciary duty applicable to municipal advisors under the federal Securities 
Exchange Act, and (iii) provide a copy of such disclosure to the municipal entity’s independent registered 
municipal advisor. GPD has provided such disclosures to WPC and its registered municipal advisor. GPD 
and Stantec individually do not provide opinions on, or advocate for using, a financial product (issuing 
debt) or the choice of financial products employed. As such, GPD and Stantec submitted its work 
products to the City’s independent registered municipal advisors Phoenix Capital Partners, LLP and 
Government Capital Management, LLC for review. 
This Report is to be used for inclusion in a preliminary official statement and a final official statement in 
connection with the issuance of the Series 2024 Bonds. Each of GPD and Stantec approves of the use of 
this Report as part of those official statements and consents to the use of its name and references to its 
company appearing in the official statements.

1.3 GPD 
GPD has been providing architecture and engineering services to public and private sector clients for 63 
years. GPD established roots in Akron, OH in 1961, and today, through the employee ownership 
structure, dedication and hard work, has grown to a firm of over 700 professionals located in 13 offices 
nationwide. Over 500 of those professionals reside in northeast Ohio, making GPD one of the largest 
design consulting firms in the area. 
GPD specializes in the areas of architectural design, engineering design, construction, 
telecommunications and environmental services. GPD services various market sectors that include public 
buildings, education, housing, healthcare, parks and recreation, water resources, power 
distribution/generation, public works, geotechnical, land surveying, federal, environmental, transportation, 
retail/commercial, site development and utility services. 
GPD’s strength rests in the ability to adapt to an ever-changing market. GPD’s leadership is committed to 
developing business within the framework of specialized market sectors, all of which contribute to the 
company’s stability in the long-term. With this strategy, GPD continues to grow and remain stable as an 
area leader and “the firm of choice” in providing professional services. GPD has provided engineering 
and professional services in various capacities to WPC since 2017. 
 
1.4 Stantec 
 
Stantec Inc is a sustainable engineering, architecture, and environmental consulting company. The 
company is geographically diversified in three regional operating units namely Canada, United States 
and Global, offering similar services across all regions. Stantec has more than 30,000 employees 
working in over 400 locations across 6 continents. Stantec offers services in various sectors across the 
project life cycle through five business operating units: infrastructure, water, buildings, environmental 
services, and energy and resources. Stantec provides professional services in all phases of the project 
life cycle: planning, design, construction administration, commissioning, maintenance, decommissioning, 
and remediation. 
 
1.5 Conventions and Acronyms 
WPC’s fiscal year begins on January 1st and ends December 31st. Throughout the Report, a fiscal year 
is identified as “FY” which is the same as the calendar year in which the fiscal year ends, i.e., FY2024 is 
the fiscal year from January 1, 2024, through December 31, 2024. 
Appendix A contains a listing of acronyms or abbreviations of terms used in the Report. These terms 
appear in multiple sections of this Report and are thus defined in Appendix A for reference.gpdgroup.com

2 ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT 
2.1 Division of Water Pollution Control 
WPC is a division of the City’s Department of Public Utilities that provides retail wastewater and 
stormwater collection services to customers in the City. WPC was established by the City on June 21, 
1965, by Ordinance 1599-65. WPC was created for the purpose of supplying sewer services to customers 
within the Cleveland metropolitan area. Beginning with a rudimentary system in the late 1800s, the 
Cleveland sewer system developed as the City itself expanded. Until the early 1970s, the City operated 
the entire system and managed all aspects of sewage treatment and disposal. 
In 1972, in response to a lawsuit brought on by several communities in Cuyahoga County, a court order 
created the Northeast Ohio Regional Sewer District (NEORSD). The ownership and operation of all 
wastewater treatment plants, interceptors, sewer regulator systems and devices, weirs, retaining basins, 
and some storm water handling facilities were transferred to NEORSD in December 1973. The City 
retained responsibility for the local sewer collection system within the City of Cleveland  corporation limits. 
Over the years, various clarifications and agreements have modified the ownership responsibility of the 
City and NEORSD with respect to the assets located within the City’s boundaries. The WPC System in 
2024 includes approximately 1,444 miles of sewers, 11 pump stations, and related appurtenances. 
In accordance with its governing legislation, WPC operates, maintains and manages the System on 
behalf of the City pursuant to the requirements of the City’s Community Discharge Permits (both the 
Community Combined Discharge Permit and Community Discharge Permit). The System transfers 
collected wastewater from its point of collection to a NEORSD interceptor sewer or treatment plant for 
processing. WPC is also charged with municipal regulation of the City’s local stormwater system and also 
regulates matters related to the pollution of watercourses. 
 
2.2 Organization Structure 
An overview of the current organization structure of WPC is shown on Figure 2-1. WPC is one of five (5) 
Divisions within the Department of Public Utilities of the City, which also includes Water, Fiscal Control, 
Radio Communications and Cleveland Public Power. WPC is managed by the Commissioner of WPC, 
pursuant to the Charter and ordinances of the City, and under the direction of the Director of Public 
Utilities. 
The WPC Commissioner reports to the Director of Public Utilities, who is appointed by the Mayor and 
reports to the Chief Operating Officer. The Board of Control, consisting of the Mayor and directors of 
several departments, establishes the rate and charges for WPC, subject to City Council approval. City 
Council is the final governing body for approval of rates and charges and other matters related to WPC.  
Several other City divisions both within and outside of the Department of Public Utilities also provide 
support services to WPC including, but not limited to, the Division of Fiscal Control for financial 
management and Division of Water for billing and collection services. Figure 2-1 shows WPC’s 
organization chart for FY2024.

WPC’s 2024 personnel budget includes 165 full-time, 6 apprentice sewer service workers, and 2 student 
assistant apprentice positions. Of these, 126 full-time and 1 apprentice positions were filled as of 
December 2023. The approved staffing level for WPC’s FY2024 personnel budget is 173, and WPC is in 
the process of hiring additional operation, maintenance and engineering staff to fill vacancies within the 
personnel budget to meet future needs. The Department has a centralized oversight of functions such as 
finance, public affairs, geographic information systems (GIS), information technology (IT), and human 
resource support budget personnel that are in the Division of Fiscal Control and Director’s Office. The 
centralized services enhance efficiency and coordination throughout the department.  
All of the full-time and apprentice employees of WPC are employees of the City, and approximately eighty-
five percent (85%) belong to one of several collective bargaining units represented within the City. There 
are currently 19 employees of 26 budgeted positions that do not belong to unions.  They include primarily 
upper level professionals and administrators. All of the union contracts are effective from April 1, 2022 
through March 31, 2025 . The City has settled with eight of the nine Unions representing 81% of the 
workforce. The City and  the remaining Union continue to negotiate the outstanding agreement. At this 
time, WPC does not anticipate that the new contracts will have any material impact on WPC’s operations 
or financial projections. 
The organizational structure of WPC generally is consistent with that of wastewater utilities in many large 
cities. The proposed staffing plan adequately addresses all of the core functions of WPC, with certain key 
support services provided by other City divisions. WPC is in the process of filling vacancies to expand its 
in-house staff and capabilities to meet future needs. 
WPC does have on-call professional services and other contracts in place to provide emergency and 
other required services as may be needed to supplement current staff availability and experience.  
 
2.3 Management Staff 
The WPC Commissioner is responsible for all technical and administrative operations of WPC, and the 
implementation of programs, policies and procedures at the direction of the Director of Public Utilities , 
Chief Operating Officer, and the Mayor. Mrs. Ramona Lowery-Ferrell currently serves as the 
Commissioner of WPC, and has served in this position since July 2020. Prior to this, Mrs. Lowery-Ferrell 
served as Deputy Commissioner for five (5) years. Mr. Martin Keane is the Director of Public Utilities, and 
began serving in this position in January 2021. Mr. Ryan Lopez is a new Deputy Commissioner for WPC, 
and reports to the Commissioner. Although Mr. Lopez started in this position in 2023, he has been an 
employee of the City since 2009. Ms. Catherine Troy, Chief Financial Officer (CFO) of the Department of 
Public Utilities, is responsible for the financial management of WPC.

2.4 Interactions with the City 
WPC is a division within the Department of Public Utilities of the City. The City also maintains several 
other departments and divisions that provide key administrative and support services to WPC. The costs 
of these interdepartmental services are charged back to WPC, as WPC operations are financed primarily 
through direct charges to the users of its services. Several of these other divisions, such as the Division 
of Water, also function as separate utilities. WPC’s allocable share of support services costs from these 
departments and divisions is based on the cost of the services provided. The interdepartmental services 
provided to WPC include but are not limited to the following: 
• Billing and collection services; 
• Procurement, accounts payable, and payroll services; 
• Budget, capital improvement planning and bond issuance support; 
• Technology Management Services (IT) and GIS support; 
• Human Resources support for hiring, terminations, and retirements; 
• Legal support for agreements by WPC and claims for and against WPC; 
• Utilities administration; 
• Motor vehicle maintenance; and 
• Street construction maintenance and repair. 
In FY2021 and FY2022, WPC paid the City approximately $4.7 million and $5.1 million, respectively, in 
interdepartmental charges, of which $2.8 million and $3.1 million were charges from Division of Water for 
billing and collection services. In FY2023, the interdepartmental charges were $5.6 million, of which $3.6 
million was for billing and collection services. Increases from previous years were partially associated with 
implementation of an automated meter (AMR) reading program, which served to increase revenues 
through elimination of estimated bills and improved reading accuracy. The Division of Water charges are 
anticipated to stay somewhat steady in FY2024, and the WPC budget will maintain an estimated $6.2 
million in interdepartmental charges for FY2024 to account for charges associated with utilities 
administration and fiscal control. These charges include WPC’s prorated share of costs associated with 
support services including public affairs, GIS, IT and finance. 
Several City divisions retain independent stormwater responsibilities that are coordinated by WPC. For example, 
the City Department of Building and Housing has the responsibility for construction and post- construction 
stormwater runoff control and the City Department of Public Works is responsible for stormwater housekeeping.  
 
2.5 Overview of Service Contracts and Agreements 
 
WPC performs operation management services required for the System using WPC staff and resources 
from other City departments and divisions. There are several Memoranda of Understanding (MOUs), on-
call service contracts and interactions with other agencies that contribute to and supplement the delivery 
of services. These include, but are not limited to: 
 
• Cuyahoga Soil and Water Conservation District, Euclid Creek Watershed Council and the Doan Brook 
Watershed Partnership regarding provisions of public involvement and public education for stormwater 
management. 
• Task-based emergency services contracts with various private vendors to assist in conducting 
emergency repairs beyond WPC’s staff resources. gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 2-6 
 
 
 
• Professional services contracts that provide general engineering services to WPC. These services 
include wastewater system design and planning, sewer system design, regulatory support, bidding 
and construction services, surveying, field representation and operations support.  
 
In addition to the above, WPC coordinates service delivery with requirements set forth by NEORSD; both 
a condition of the City’s discharge permits and as the host community for a significant portion of 
NEORSD’s assets as detailed below. 
 
2.6 NEORSD Interactions 
Prior to 1973, the City was responsible for both wastewater collection and wastewater treatment. 
Beginning in December 1973, the ownership and operation of all of the wastewater treatment plants, 
interceptors, sewer regulator systems and devices, weirs, retaining basins, and some stormwater facilities 
were transferred to NEORSD pursuant to a 1972 Court Order. Subsequently, further clarification of the 
maintenance responsibilities of NEORSD-related collection system assets located within the City limits, 
was confirmed by an Agreed Judgment Entry and Court Order (City of Cleveland v NEORSD, Cuyahoga 
County Court of Common Pleas, Case #455021) dated April 2005. This order specified that certain 
assets should be maintained by NEORSD as part of its interceptor system and not maintained by the 
City. An MOU and Exhibits A-C that are attached to the Agreed Judgment Entry and Appendix D, Figure 
D-1 (which is attached to this Report), provides maps and a description of the assets owned and 
maintained by each of the parties under the 2005 Agreed Judgment Entry and the Court Orders. The 
clarifications included confirmation, among other items, that the regulator weirs are the responsibility of 
NEORSD. 
Updates to the allocation of responsibilities are conducted as needed by mutual agreement between  
WPC and NEORSD. This included the transfer of the Mary Street, West 11 th Street and Stones Levee 
pump stations from WPC to NEORSD in 2016. In 2021, WPC’s Service Yard pump station was 
decommissioned as a result of a NEORSD project. NEORSD and WPC also finalized a stormwater 
agreement in 2016, which under the authority of Title V of NEORSD’s Stormwater Management Code, 
transferred the operation and maintenance of the assets that are part of the Regional Stormwater System, 
including two of WPC’s detention basins, from WPC to NEORSD. These transfers of responsibility are 
consistent with NEORSD’s Project Clean Lake Long Term Control Plan (LTCP), as required by USEPA, 
and Regional Stormwater Management Program (RSMP) goals. These transfers are by prior mutual 
agreement between WPC and NEORSD because NEORSD has identified these assets as key 
components for NEORSD’s systems. These assets are not considered part of the System, and were not 
included in the condition assessment review conducted by GPD as part of the preparation of this Report. 
WPC’s discharge of flows to NEORSD’s interceptor system for transfer and treatment at NEORSD’s 
wastewater treatment plants is governed by the Community Combined Discharge Permit with NEORSD 
(dated March 1995) for combined sewers (NEORSD Title IV code) and the Community Discharge Permit 
with NEORSD (dated June 1986) for sanitary sewer (NEORSD Title III code). Neither permit has an 
expiration date. However, the permits may be modified if necessary to reflect the changing needs and  
circumstances of NEORSD and WPC. NEORSD is currently working with WPC to provide a single 
updated permit in lieu of the two active discharge permits. In addition, there are several levels of 
coordination between WPC and NEORSD as summarized below: 
 
 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 2-7 
 
 
• Monthly NEORSD engineering coordination meetings with WPC engineering participation. 
• Quarterly NEORSD Water Action Committee meetings with WPC representation participation. 
• NEORSD Project Clean Lake LTCP Implementation and Coordination with City on green 
infrastructure projects, problem area studies and flow monitoring. 
• City representation as voting members on NEORSD governing board. Of the 7 members of 
NEORSD governing board, 3 are appointed by the City. 
• NEORSD inclusion in the approval process and requirements for any new development and 
redevelopment within the City. 
WPC consistently complies with its Community Discharge Permit and Community Combined Discharge 
Permit (collectively referred to as the Community Discharge Permits), requirements that require 
implementation of a number of best management practices. Under the Community Discharge Permit for 
the sanitary sewer system, a peak flow limitation of 14.284 million gallons per day (MGDs) is imposed at 
the Grayton Road connector. WPC and NEORSD have not reported any exceedance of this limitation.  
With respect to the Community Combined Discharge Permit, there are no peak flow limits or restrictions 
imposed at this time. However, NEORSD has the right to impose future limitations, if required. No 
limitations are anticipated to be imposed within the forecast period as (a) the quantity of wastewater flow 
currently delivered to NEORSD is less today than it was when the discharge permits were issued, (b) the 
System is essentially fully developed, and (c) the City maintains a policy of managing the amount o f 
wastewater flow discharged to its collection system to result in peak flow rates at, or less than, what the 
System was designed for. In addition, as part of its LTCP required by USEPA consistent with a consent 
decree, NEORSD has been and is undertaking improvements for handling wet weather flows, including 
tunnels for capture and storage of peak wastewater flows. The City is not a party to the consent decree. 
In addition to wastewater treatment responsibility, NEORSD also supports the WPC stormwater 
management program. On September 15, 2015, the Ohio Supreme Court ruled that NEORSD was 
authorized to implement its RSMP and had authority to charge stormwater fees  to fund it. Under the 
RSMP, NEORSD collects stormwater management fees from its customers using the City as its billing 
agent. Use of the funds is authorized by Ohio Revised Code (ORC) Chapter 6119 for the use, services or 
benefits of a water-resource project afforded to property owners in its defined regional stormwater area, 
including in the City. That includes NEORSD assuming responsibility for millions of dollars of necessary 
maintenance along streams across the region. NEORSD began assessing and collecting the RSMP 
charges in 2016. Pursuant to NEORSD’s RSMP’s Community Cost Share policy, member communities 
are eligible to receive funding for qualifying community-specific stormwater projects pursuant to an 
application process. A minimum of twenty-five percent (25%) of the total annual stormwater fee revenue 
collected in each member community is allocated to the Community Cost-Share Account for each 
member community. The City is in regular communication with NEORSD regarding the RSMP and 
Community Cost Share funding opportunities.gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 3-1 
 
 
 
3 SYSTEM DESCRIPTION 
3.1 Service Area 
The City is located in the northeastern part of the State of Ohio on the southern shore of Lake Erie, 
approximately sixty (60) miles west of the Pennsylvania border. The City is the county seat of Cuyahoga 
County, which is the second most populous county in the State of Ohio. The City itself is the second 
largest city in the State of Ohio, covering a land surface area of 77.7 square miles. It is geographically 
located near the mouth of the Cuyahoga River, and extends along the southern shore of Lake Erie. 
Founded in 1796, the City is a diverse city of approximately 361,607 people (based on 2022 U.S. Census 
Bureau statistics). 
WPC’s service area includes all areas within the City limits and represents a significant portion of the 
entire metropolitan area tributary to NEORSD interceptor and treatment plants. The service area includes 
residential, commercial, industrial, government and non-profit customers located with the City limits. It 
also includes customers that may be physically outside the City limits, but have their water meters located 
within the City limits. A map of WPC’s service area and the types of sewer service provided within the 
service area is shown in Appendix D Figure D-2. 
 
3.2 Description of Major Assets 
WPC is responsible for operating and maintaining its wastewater and stormwater collection systems. The 
System currently includes 11 pump stations, approximately 1,444 miles of both sanitary and stormwater 
collection systems (mostly in the form of a combined sewer collection system), 36,000 manholes, 43,500 
catch basins/storm drains, and related equipment as discussed further below. 
 
3.2.1 WPC Sewer Collection System 
WPC’s System includes three (3) types of sewers. The most prevalent type are sewer pipes that collect 
and transfer combined sanitary and storm flows. The System also includes separate sanitary and storm 
sewers, and a third category of segmented block pipes wherein separate sanitary and storm sewers are 
encased within the same segmented block pipe. Appendix D, Figure D-2 provides a map that illustrates 
the areas within the City served by combined and separated (including segmented block) sewers. As 
illustrated in Appendix D Figure D-2, the majority of the System is served by combined sewers. 
Ninety percent (90%) of the sewers have an equivalent pipe diameter of less than sixty (60) inches; the most  
common pipe size being twelve (12) inches in diameter. The most common pipe material (fifty-three percent 
(53%)) is vitrified clay pipe (VCP), but with significant quantities of both brick (twenty -six percent (26%)) and 
reinforced concrete pipe (eighteen percent (18%)). The segmented block pipe accounts for approximately 
42,000 linear feet or less than one percent (1%) of the total linear feet of pipe. There is an additional two 
percent (2%) of the system classified as "Other" which includes materials such as ductile iron . The majority of 
the System was constructed in the 1920s and before and is estimated to have an average age of 
approximately eighty-four (84) years. Portions of the System date back to the 1870s. Figure 3-1 presents a 
graphical summary of WPC’s collection system by age and type. Since 2000, WPC has installed over 
171,000 feet of pipe. 
 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 3-2 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-1. WPC Sewer Pipe Age and Type 
The estimated useful life of a typical sewer is approximately one hundred (100) years, and approximately 
thirty-four percent (34%) of WPC’s collection system is over one hundred (100) years old. WPC 
performed a comprehensive capital improvement program from 2017-2023, which included conducting a 
Sewer System Evaluation Survey (SSES), as part of addressing this aging infrastructure. The SSES 
identified and prioritized potential sewer improvement projects with an estimated construction valve of 
$260 million. 
WPC is responsible for the operation and maintenance of the System within the City limits, including from 
the main sewer up to one (1) foot from the curb line of the roadway. The house connections (laterals) and 
connections from the building to one (1) foot from the curb line are the responsibility of the building 
owners. The interceptors and regulators that transfer flow from the WPC collection system to NEORSD 
treatment facilities are the responsibility of NEORSD as described in Section 2 and are therefore not 
included as WPC assets. 
 
3.2.2 Stormwater System 
The City’s stormwater system consists primarily of approximately forty-three thousand five hundred 
(43,500) catch basins, which lead primarily to the combined sewer collection system and approximately 
two hundred six (206) miles of separated storm sewers. A significant portion of these separated storm 
sewers are within a segmented block pipe installed in the 1920s. Separate storm and separate sanitary 
flows are channeled within their respective open channel pipe, both enclosed within the same casing. 
Any storm sewer flows in excess of the pipe’s capacity will overflow into the sanitary system. 
The City’s stormwater system is operated and maintained pursuant to its Ohio Environmental Protection 
Agency, National Pollution Discharge Elimination System (NPDES) permit (NPDES No. OHQ000004) for 
Small Municipal Separate Storm Sewer Systems (Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit). The remainder of the 
City’s stormwater is managed as part of its wastewater collection system as approximately seventy-two

percent (72%) of the WPC’s System is a combined sanitary and storm sewer system. 
The WPC Commissioner serves as the City’s Stormwater Manager for implementing and coordinating the 
best management practices required as part of a stormwater management plan required under its Ohio 
EPA NPDES MS4 permit. A Stormwater Steering Committee consisting of representatives from WPC, 
Cuyahoga County Soil and Water Conservation District, the City’s Departments of Public Works, Law, 
and Building and Housing, Cleveland City Planning Commission, Office of Sustainability, Division of 
Waste Collection, Office of Capital Projects and City Council implement the stormwater management plan 
across multiple departments for compliance with the City’s Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit.  
WPC’s responsibilities include overall implementation, management, enforcement and reporting on 
stormwater compliance including compliance with the Six Minimum Control Measures as specified in the 
City’s Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit. Table 3-1 summarizes the allocation of responsibility for each of 
these Six Minimum Control Measures.

3.2.3 Sewage Pump Stations 
WPC operates and maintains eleven (11) sewage pump stations, which are utilized to transport sanitary 
waste where gravity flow is not a viable transport option. This number of pump stations does not include 
the three (3) pump stations that were transferred from WPC to NEORSD as discussed in  Section 2.6 or 
the Service Yard pump station that has been decommissioned. The remaining sewage pump stations are 
relatively small, each with less than one thousand fifty (1,050) gallons per minute (gpm) capacity. 
A listing of the wastewater pump stations is included in Section 5 of this Report. The condition of the 
pump stations is discussed in Section 5. gpdgroup.com

4 OPERATIONS AND MAINTENANCE 
4.1 Sewer Legislation 
Legal authority for WPC’s System derives from the City’s Charter and codes and ordinances, which are 
reviewed and revised as needed. All ordinances are available on the City’s website. WPC’s key  sewer 
ordinances are: City of Cleveland Code of Ordinances, Part 5 – Municipal Utilities and Services Code, 
Title VII – Sewers and Sewage Disposal, Chapter 541- Sewer Connections and Sewer Use Code; 
Charter reference § 163. 
This statutory framework regulates the use of public and private sewers and drains in the City Right -of- 
Way. It also requires, among other things, that individual property owners maintain their sewer service 
connections from one foot beyond the curb to the property to be drained and control unacceptable 
discharges to the collection system, including natural watercourses. If a blockage is in the main sewer or 
between the main sewer and a point one foot beyond the existing or proposed curb line of the roadwa y, 
repairs shall be made by WPC. If the blockage is between the building and one (1) foot beyond the 
existing or proposed curb line of the roadway, WPC advises the owner to engage a licensed and bonded 
sewer contractor to make the necessary repairs. 
WPC also complies with its obligations under its Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit and NEORSD’s Code of 
Regulations, Title III “Separate Sanitary Sewer Code” and Title IV “Combined Sewer Code” and 
associated Community Combined Sewer Discharge Permit and Community Discharge Permit.  
 
4.2 Sewer Extensions and Connections 
The System is fully developed. Should new connections to the existing collection system or 
redevelopment projects by developers be proposed, approval by WPC, Cleveland City Planning 
Commission and the Department of Building and Housing, as well as NEORSD is required. In addition, 
pursuant to City policy, the connections must maintain compliance with WPC’s policy of managing  the 
amount of wastewater flow discharged to its collection system as previously discussed in Section 2.6. 
 
4.3 Operation and Maintenance 
WPC maintains a sewer system preventative maintenance program that includes implementation of best 
management practices. Historically, WPC’s program focused first on addressing any complaints and 
known problem areas and second on conducting preventative maintenance. While these priorities remain, 
WPC continues to increase both its preventative maintenance efforts and resources and rate of 
replacement of sewers which are past their useful life. WPC continues to prioritize preventative 
maintenance investment and reduce potential for sanitary sewer overflows (SSOs) in a cost -effective 
manner. Key elements of the program include: 
• Using Cityworks, a computerized maintenance and management system (CMMS), to track all 
activities including inspections, cleaning, and rehabilitation and replacement projects.  
• Continual updating and maintaining of WPC sewer maps in GIS. 
• Conducting condition assessments in order to budget and plan for upcoming rehabilitation and 
replacement programs. 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 4-2 
 
 
• Maintaining an adequate inventory of equipment and spare parts to allow immediate response to 
emergencies and prevent sanitary sewer overflows. 
• Developing Standard Operating Procedures (SOPs). 
• Establishing performance measures and goals. 
• Expanding safety measures, equipment and training for employees. 
• Establishing operation and maintenance programs for cleaning and inspections. 
Currently, the cleaning of the sewers is done by means of a priority schedule in which blocked main 
sewers are completed first. Known problem areas that do not present an immediate concern are then 
prioritized based on available information and scheduled for cleaning. As workload permits, preventative 
maintenance cleaning is then performed. WPC has a goal of cleaning at least two hundred thousand 
(200,000) linear feet of sewer mains per year. 
WPC performs catch basin cleaning to provide a clear pathway for water to enter the sewer system. WPC 
typically cleans more than an average of approximately eleven thousand (11,000) catch basins per year. 
The schedule of catch basin cleaning is based on known problem areas, visual observation and by 
requests. WPC’s goal is to continue catch basin cleaning so that each catch basin is inspected and 
cleaned at least once every three (3) years. 
The City Division of Streets contributes to WPC preventative maintenance program by performing all of 
the street cleaning in the City. 
WPC performs routine inspections of its sewer lines through a staff of house connection inspectors who 
investigate any complaint and determine the process of resolution, including follow up. They also make 
visual inspections while on rounds to detect problems in the sewer lines including (among other items) 
observations of sunken or flooded areas, ground cracks along pipe routes, damage to special structures 
and manholes, manhole covers above or below pavement levels, and evidence of flooding or seepage 
from sewers. This information is recorded in the City’s Cityworks application which is also used to log 
customer service requests and complaints and to generate and manage maintenance work orders and 
performance measures. 
Based on the available information, WPC’s maintenance crews are deployed to determine the problem 
and to perform necessary repairs. Cleaning and closed circuit television (CCTV) inspections are 
conducted. and a permanent video log of the inspections is kept for further study. WPC promptly repairs 
problems with in-house staff. Maintenance crews are dispatched by a priority rating system with 
customer “no relief” status given the highest priority. For large scale emergencies, WPC employs on-call 
contractors to insure immediate repairs. 
WPC received 4,861 sewer service requests in 2021 and 4,894 requests in 2022. In 2023, approximately 
4,689 sewer service requests were recorded and investigated by WPC. Sewer flow issues, including 
sewer back up and flooding, accounted for approximately thirty-six percent (36%) of these calls. Of the 
complaints received, many were attributable to poor private lateral conditions. Typically, the private 
laterals account for the majority of the poor sewer conditions due to lack of maintenance on the  
homeowners’ part as well as the increased vulnerability of the laterals due to  age and root intrusion. The 
remainder of the complaints were to report, or to request, an investigation related to blocked catch basins, 
missing or broken manhole covers, sewer cave-ins, or damaged catch basins. 
 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 4-3 
 
 
Some of the calls or requests were to report an illicit discharge to the System, such as a chemical spill, 
septage discharge or the presence of chemical odors. Some calls were for illegal dumping into the 
System such as dumping of debris. 
The classifications of calls and requests are generally consistent from year to year. After the receipt of a 
call or a service request, WPC issues a work order for investigation of the request. The work order may  
be followed by several other work orders based on the need for the particular investigation. A service call 
may generate more than three work orders depending on the situation.  
WPC reports on its maintenance performance to NEORSD on an annual basis. Table 4-1 provides a 
summary of WPC’s key maintenance performance measures since 2019.

As illustrated above, the performance for any line item varies from year to year depending on the priority 
needs of the System which, in turn, are affected by customer service requests, weather conditions, 
emergencies and availability of equipment. Overall, the goals set by WPC are achieved on average by 
responding to service requests and filling in with proactive work. 
WPC also maintains records relating to the a) average number of days to complete house connection 
repair work; b) average time to address missing manhole covers; and c) average response time for 
customer service complaints. WPC uses this information to benchmark and improve future performance. 
On average, WPC responds to customer complaints within forty-seven (47) minutes and completes repair 
work within three (3) days. 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 4-4 
 
 
WPC also conducts regular inspection of its pump stations, including performing preventative 
maintenance and repair and replacement of components as needed for proper operations and to maintain 
redundancy. The Pump Station Supervisory Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) system monitors pump 
and other equipment operations and performance and alerts the Pump Station Section staff of alarms. The 
Pump Station Section staff can immediately respond to alarms during the typical work shift, and within 
hours during off-hours. 
WPC’s historical operation and maintenance efforts have served to fulfill customer requests and meet the 
immediate needs of the System. WPC has established a proactive approach to meet both the current and 
long-term needs of the System. To assist in implementing this approach, WPC received approval for the 
implementation of a new fixed charge component to the sewer rate beginning in FY2015. A  discussion of 
the rates and charges is presented in Section 7 of this Report. The fixed charge was established to 
provide for increased investment in infrastructure, which facilitates replacement of aging sewers and 
related equipment. This investment is intended to result in fewer emergency repairs and an increase in 
proactive cleaning and inspections. 
 
4.4 Key Operating Permits and Regulatory Status 
Regulations affecting the collection and discharge of wastewater from WPC are contained in the City’s 
Community Discharge Permits with NEORSD and its Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit. NEORSD discharge 
permits regulate the sanitary and the combined sanitary and storm sewers. These permits do not contain 
any expiration date, but are subject to modifications as necessary. Pursuant to these discharge permits, 
WPC is required to submit an Annual Compliance Report Checklist to NEORSD. The compliance report 
checklist demonstrates WPC’s compliance with the permit and NEORSD’s best management practice 
(BMP) guidelines. NEORSD’s BMP guidelines contain five (5) key BMPs: 
• BMP 1: General Conditions 
• BMP 2: Operation and Maintenance and Inspection Program 
• BMP 3: Source Control 
• BMP 4: Record System 
• BMP 5: BMP Frequencies 
o Sewer System Cleaning 
o Sewer System Inspection 
o Catch Basin Cleaning 
o Street Sweeping 
o Storage Device Cleaning 
o Leaf Pickup 
o Pump Station Operationsgpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 4-5 
 
 
Discharge of stormwater for WPC is regulated by the City’s Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit. As discussed 
in Section 4, only a small portion of the System consists of separated storm sewers. The majority of the 
System is combined storm and sanitary sewer system that is regulated under the previously discussed 
discharge permits. The most recent Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit was issued on April 1, 2021. Key 
requirements of the permit include compliance with the Six Minimum Control Measures and submission of 
annual reports to the Ohio Environmental Protection Agency (OEPA or Ohio EPA) on compliance 
activities as previously discussed in Section 3.2.2. Additionally, the permit requires the City to develop 
and implement the Total Maximum Daily Load (TMDL) Performance Standards for MS4 discharges to 
Euclid Creek regarding total phosphorous as a minimum. 
 
4.4.1 Regulatory Compliance 
WPC is not under any administrative orders, and to the best of our knowledge, there are no issued 
notices of violations. NEORSD, however, is under a Consent Order to eliminate combined sewer 
overflows and improve water quality in Lake Erie through the implementation of its LTCP.  
WPC is implementing NEORSD BMPs included in its permits and reporting on its compliance to NEORSD 
through submission of its Annual Compliance Report Checklist as required by the permits. WPC is also 
supporting NEORSD in implementing its LTCP. The BMPs included in WPC’s discharge permits provide 
various BMP frequency guidelines. These guidelines represent goals for WPC, and are not a condition or 
requirement of the permits. 
With respect to the Ohio EPA NPDES MS4 permit for stormwater, WPC documents its compliance 
activities in its annual reports to the Ohio EPA. Based on a review of these reports, WPC appears to be in 
compliance with the requirements. 
At this time, there are no other known regulations forecasted to be promulgated in the near future that 
would have a significant impact on WPC’s operations, or would require significant capital expenditures in 
addition to those already anticipated by WPC. As a condition of WPC’s discharge permit, NEORSD has 
the ability to impose peak flow limits, which could require significant capital investment in excess of 
currently contemplated capital expenditures. At this time, there is no indication that NEORSD would 
impose any such limits. See Section 2.6 regarding WPC’s coordination with NEORSD. 
 
4.5 Discussion 
WPC maintains a sewer system preventative maintenance program that includes implementation of 
BMPs and is in compliance with its regulatory permits. As part of these BMPs, the permits identify key 
goals and frequencies to measure BMP achievements. These goals and frequencies are not 
requirements, but guidelines. WPC has established System BMP goals that appear to appropriately 
balance costs and benefits and be a sound basis for measurement of these goals. Historically, WPC’s 
program focused first on addressing any complaints and known problem areas and second on conducting 
preventative maintenance. While these priorities remain, WPC is increasing its resources (both through 
the fixed charge to fund capital, and through filling staffing vacancies for maintenance and repairs) to 
provide for increased preventative maintenance and a higher rate of replacement of sewers and 
equipment that are past their useful lives. WPC’s continued investment in capital and filling staffing 
vacancies will allow WPC to progress towards achieving the BMP goals. gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-1 
 
 
5 CONDITION OF SYSTEM 
5.1 Condition Assessment Approach 
GPD conducted a review of the System in March 2024 in connection with the proposed issuance of the 
Series 2024 Bonds. The review included a review of the Pump Station Condition Assessment Report 
(updated September 10, 2021) and a desk-top review of available information regarding the age and 
condition of buried infrastructure (linear assets) and equipment used to maintain the System. The review 
of the System also included a review of existing reports provided by WPC, and discussions and 
interviews with WPC staff. The key purpose of this System review was to confirm anticipated conditions, 
assess general maintenance and repair of the System, and assess the adequacy of WPC’s proposed 
capital improvement plan. 
The critical assets of WPC include eleven (111) pump stations. The System review focused primarily on 
the reported condition of these pump stations as described in the 2021 Pump Station Condition 
Assessment Report. In discussions and interviews with WPC’s pump station operations and maintenance 
staff, issues facing WPC were discussed including the steps being taken to address these issues. The 
condition of the pump stations and ancillary equipment also was discussed. Key findings are discussed in 
subsequent sections. 
For buried infrastructure (i.e., sewer interceptors, storm sewers, sanitary and combined sewers, laterals, 
force mains, manholes, catch basins, etc.) and the maintenance equipment, the System review relied on 
existing information provided by WPC. This included information regarding the type, age , and reported 
condition of maintenance equipment and preliminary GIS information regarding the age and materials of 
pipes (to the extent that information is available), as well as reports made available regarding compliance 
activities. No field investigations were conducted on the buried infrastructure or maintenance equipment. 
GPD has no reason to question the soundness of the information provided by WPC based on historical 
knowledge of the System, and as such, accepted the information as provided.  
The condition assessment of the critical above-ground assets was based on numerical ratings within the 
following categories. 
• Reliability 
• Operations and maintenance (O&M) performance 
• Capacity 
• Regulatory Compliance 
Using the above categories, an overall risk rating was assigned to each of the pump stations. The risk 
ratings for each of the above four categories are outlined in Table 5-1. The risk rating assigned to each 
pump station is presented in Section 5.2 following the description of the pump stations. 
 
 
 
1 WPC was originally responsible for 15 pump stations. However, three have been transferred to NEORSD as discussed in Section

When considering the rating assigned to a critical asset, the criticality of that asset was also considered. 
For assets that are critical to the overall operation and regulatory compliance of the facility, the allowable 
risk is small. Thus, highly critical assets would tend to be given more stringent ratings.  
 
5.2 Condition of Sewage Pumping Stations 
In general, based on our review of the Pump Station Condition Assessment Report, it appears that most 
of WPC’s pumping stations are in fair to good physical condition, fairly reliable, and have adequate 
hydraulic capacity. Appendix D Figure D-3 provides a map that shows the relative location of WPC’s pump 
stations within System. The following provides a description of the pump stations and their risk review 
rating. 
WPC utilizes two distinct types of sewage pumping stations. All the stations are duplex stations, either the  
centrifugal suction lift type or the centrifugal flooded suction type. Some of the suction lift stations are 
housed in an above-ground fiberglass enclosure with the pumping equipment, controls, valves and piping 
mounted over a precast concrete wet well. All of the centrifugal flooded suction type stations and some of 
the suction lift stations utilize a pre-fabricated “can” station, which includes a dry well containing the 
pumps, controls and valves and a separate wet well that collects the wastewater before pumping.  These 
stations require certified personnel to enter the dry wells with specialized equipment due to confined 
space entry requirements. 
All of WPC’s pump stations are relatively small in size (under 1,050 gallons per minute capacity), with 
pump inlets of either four (4) inch or six (6) inch suction pipe size. With the exception of the Burke 
Lakefront Airport Pump Station, East 37th Street Pump Station, and the Incinerator Pump Station, most 
were constructed in the late 1980s and 1990s and are within the expected design  lives for the facilities. 
WPC has no ownership responsibilities of the Burke Lakefront Airport Pump Station. Another City 
department retains such ownership, but WPC is responsible for operations and maintenance of the pump 
station. gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-3 
 
 
WPC pump stations are generally reliable and no major issues are reported. The Incinerator Pump 
Station was replaced in 2015, and the force main was replaced in 2018. The East 37th Street Pump 
Station was replaced with all new components and a new force main in 2020. The remaining stations that 
are forty (40) or more years old, such as the pump stations located at Nottingham Road, Panna Lane,  
Burke Airport, and Harsax Avenue, will require significant upgrades/replacement in future years. Plans 
for the replacement of the Panna Lane station have been completed as of this report . The project is 
budgeted for construction in FY2025. 
WPC’s pumping stations have two pumps, only one of which is required for normal peak conditions. 
However, during severe wet weather events both pumps may need to operate and may still not meet 
peak conditions due to excessive inflow and infiltration (I/I) into the System. WPC also has portable 
pumps that are transported and set up at pump stations to assist with pumping the flow during wet 
weather events as needed. The vast majority of pumping stations have adequate, and even excess , 
capacity and thus run infrequently. Table 5-2 identifies the run times of the pump stations under average 
day dry weather conditions.

For the Spring Hill and the Incinerator Pump Stations, WPC utilizes a special design of its duplex 
pumping systems, which consists of one of the two duplex pumps being equipped with both an electric 
motor, as well as an internal combustion engine operating on natural gas. This engine driven pump, when 
operated during a power loss event, produces adequate power to operate the system controls including 
telemetry and operate a single pump to maintain system operations. Because these pump stations are 
equipped with engine driven pumps, back-up generators are not required. For the remaining stations, 
WPC also can provide portable pumps or generators during power outages to provide capacity and 
power to the affected pump stations. Moving forward, WPC has included in their pump station design 
standards that above-ground stations are preferred for ease of maintenance and safety. Additionally, new 
designs shall include on-site generators to supply automatic backup power to the stations via natural gas-
powered generators during loss of utility power events. gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-4 
 
 
WPC has two maintenance contracts in place to provide service and repair on all of its pump stations. 
These service contracts appear to be effective at maintaining the pump stations and associated telemetry 
and control systems since each are in good working condition. 
All of the stations are equipped with telemetry to remotely monitor the pump stations and transmit critical 
signals and alarms to WPC’s Supervisory Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) system. Should the iFix 
cellular-based communication system be interrupted, WPC dispatches crews to monitor the pump 
stations on a daily basis. 
While many of the pumping stations are in residential neighborhoods, WPC does not report any

pump stations to be replaced over the next 5-10 years.

stations as needed. Details of the 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan are provided in Section 6. 
 
5.3 Condition of the Collection System 
As presented in Section 3 of this Report, the estimated average age of the  WPC collection system is 
eighty-four (84) years, and it is estimated that approximately thirty-four percent (34%) of the system is 
older than one hundred (100) years. 
Over the past five (5) years, WPC has invested approximately $11.3 million per year for its capital 
program, which includes renewal and replacement of its aging infrastructure. Recognizing that a 
consistent to increased level of investment is needed to preserve the long-term reliability of its assets, 
WPC strives to increase targeted investment in renewal and replacement. During FY2016-FY2020, 
WPC’s operating budget included $1 million each year for a five (5) year period to conduct sewer system 
evaluation surveys (SSESs) to identify sewer pipes in need of repair and replacement. The SSES 
included infrastructure inspections, condition assessment, flow monitoring, smoke and dye testing and 
associated rehabilitation recommendations, including criticality evaluation of the risks and challenges. In 
addition, WPC’s capital budget, which was increased to an average of $12.8 million per year for that five 
(5) year period, included the hiring of a Program Manager (PMT project) to assist WPC in the 
implementation of its targeted capital plan. That capital plan represented more than a doubling of 
historical investment into the System. The PMT project resulted in improved capital project planning and 
implementation practices, designs with ~$150 million in construction value, and identified ~$250 million in 
additional potential projects. Section 6 presents additional details regarding WPC’s 2024-2028 Capital 
Improvement Plan forecast. 
WPC is serving its customers and maintaining compliance with its permit requirements.  A review of 
customer service requests indicates that approximately ten percent (10%) of the requests are potentially 
associated with collapses and breaks due to deteriorated pipe conditions. WPC conducts CCTV 
inspection of pipes to examine sewers/connections as these service requests are called in by residents.  
The number of service requests and repairs have remained fairly consistent over the past five (5) years. 
Generally, WPC is able to address service requests, then move on to proactive work necessary to keep up 
with increased requirements associated with the aging infrastructure.  
WPC also undertakes ongoing condition assessments, as well  as contracting for professional 
engineering services to conduct comprehensive  studies which include such activities as flow 
monitoring, modeling, and providing recommendations for  known problem areas. The most recent of 
studies and reports include a 2021 memorandum titled “E ast 185th Street and Marcella Road Relief 
Sewer – Post Construction Monitoring Memorandum”, a 2020 study titled “Lee Road Area Sewer 
Study – Hydraulic Modeling Summary”, and a 2021 memorandum titled “Chatfield Area Sewer Options 
Analysis Memorandum”. Moving forward, WPC has allocated in its 2024-2028 Capital Improvement 
Plan $500,000 for FY2024 and $1 million per year during FY2025-FY2028 for General Engineering 
Services (GES) to further develop the targeted capital improvement plan.  
The East 185th Street/Lakeshore Boulevard study area is one of the few areas in  the City with 
separated sanitary and storm sewers. The local sanitary sewer system in the area conveys flow to the gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-6 
 
 
Euclid Creek Pump Station through the Lakeshore Boulevard Interceptor Sewer.  In 2015, NEORSD 
increased the capacity of its system to receive flows fro m the City’s System by upgrading the Euclid 
Creek Pump Station and constructing a new relief sewer. In 2018, a study and sewer modeling of the 
area was performed which recommended the construction of a WPC relief sewer along Marcella Road 
and East 185th Street, as well as  increases to the capacity of the upstream system to reduce the 
frequency of water -in-basement (WIB) complaints and flooding in the area. Construction of WPC’s 
$14 million relief sewer was completed in 2020, and post -construction monitoring was performed in 
2021 to verify the accuracy of the study’s model and reinforce the importance of providing increased 
capacity further upstream of the relief sewer. 
 
The Lee Road Area, between Miles Avenue and Invermere Avenue, is another area with separated 
sanitary and storm sewers. Heavy I/I has been reported in the area since 1996, and a 2008 SSES of the 
project area identified specific I/I sources that have since been corrected and rehabilitated by WPC. After 
rehabilitation in the area, frequent street flooding and WIB complaints still occurred . WPC then authorized 
a study of the area which included sewer monitoring, modeling, and recommendations. The study was 
completed in 2020 which recommended a relief sewer and improvements to the storm baffle -drop 
manholes. The 2020 study estimated $61.2 million for the construction of a relief sewer and other sewer 
improvements. Since the report completion, four storm baffle-drop manholes have been modified to 
further increase capacity within the storm system. Additionally, WPC has completed design for 3 of the 5 
proposed phases within the 2020 study, with an estimated cost of $28.4 million. This project is budgeted 
for construction in FY2025. 
 
The Chatfield Area (West 157th Street to West 161st Street between Valleyview Avenue to the south and 
Chatfield Avenue to the north) also has a history of WIB complaints which led WPC to authorize a study 
of the area after receiving results from a WPC-led questionnaire to residents in the area. The study of the 
area included capacity evaluation, and recommendations to reduce the frequency of WIB complaints in 
the area. The report was completed in 2021 and recommended the installation of a new interceptor 
sewer, and redirection of tributary sewers to convey combined flows directly to an NEORSD interceptor, 
ultimately relieving the existing overloaded trunk sewer along Chatfield Avenue. The construction cost 
was estimated at $20 million. Since the completion of the report, design of the first phase which includes 
the new interceptor sewer is nearly complete and requires final coordination with NEORSD. Th e first 
phase is estimated to cost $9.6 million. This project is budgeted for construction in FY2026. 
 
5.4 Condition of Collection System Equipment 
WPC maintains a fleet of more than eighty (80) vehicles ranging from pickup trucks used by supervisors 
and staff to conduct inspections and respond to complaints to heavy equipment used by staff to respond 
to emergencies. All of the vehicles are operational and are used daily or as needed to maintain the 
System. Figure 5-1 provides an inventory summary of the key equipment used to maintain the System. 
The equipment is in good operational condition and is available to meet the System demands.  
The average age of the vehicles and equipment is shown in Figure 5-2. WPC typically establishes a ten 
(10) year life for vehicles. The vehicles that are in poor condition are scheduled for replacement as budget 
permits. In the meantime, they serve as backup vehicles. WPC purchased four (4) new vehicles in 2023 
and budgeted an average of $3.43 million per year in its 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan for the 
purchase and/or lease of new vehicles and equipment. This $17.139 million Equipment investment in the 
2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan is anticipated to fund replacement of vehicles and equipment, including 
$7.392 million for the purchase of sewer cleaning trucks with Community Cost Share funds, to meet its vehicle gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-7 
 
 
and equipment replacement plan. 
WPC’s ability to address sewer system investigations and repairs depends on the availability of good 
operating vehicles and maintenance equipment. The current equipment replacement plan established 
should continue to further increase preventative maintenance activity, television inspections and 
completion of repairs.

5.5 Discussion 
Based on March-April 2024 review of asset data, GPD has made some observations of the System that, if 
addressed, could further improve facility performance and operations. Many of these already are 
addressed in WPC’s 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan. They include: 
• The wastewater and stormwater collection system has an average age of approximately eighty-four 
(84) years with parts of the system exceeding the typical useful life. Conducting system condition 
assessments and detailed investigation of customer service requests will help to identify priorities 
for renewal and replacement. Use of Cityworks and GIS will facilitate the development of a 
comprehensive asset management program, resulting in a cost-effective and efficient 
implementation of WPC’s capital improvement plan. WPC should further target increasing its 
capital expenditures over the long term, with a goal of renewal and replacement of one percent 
(1%) of its system per year, subject to the results of ongoing sewer inspections. This likely will 
require continued annual capital investment at least equal to that of the 2024-2028 Capital 
Improvement Plan in the future. 
• WPC conducts regular inspections and preventative maintenance of the pump stations to ensure 
operability. Several pump stations (Burke Lakefront Airport Pump Station, Nottingham Road 
Pump Station, Harsax Ave Pump Station, and Panna Lane Pump Station) are a high priority for 
renewal and replacement investment to minimize potential risks. The Panna Lane Pump Station 
is slated for replacement in FY2025. The Nottingham Road Pump Station, Harsax Avenue Pump 
Station, and Burke Lakefront Airport Pump Station likely will require replacement within the next 
5-10 years. The Bradley Road Pump Station, Stevedore Pump Station, and Spring Hill Pump 
Station will also require some rehabilitation. The cost of these improvements could be up to $7 
million. Capital improvements for the Burke Lakefront Airport Station remain the Department of 
Port Control’s responsibility. The Department of Port Control has been made aware of the need to 
replace the pump station. WPC will evaluate the refurbishment needs of these pump stations and 
implement the needed improvements over the next five 5-10 years. It is anticipated that some 
funding for the improvements may come from WPC’s Unspecified Projects allocation contained in 
its 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan. 
• The allocation of capital funds established for maintenance equipment appears sufficient to meet 
the needs of the System. The 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan addresses these 
requirements. 
Overall, the System appears to have sufficient capacity to meet current and projected future wastewater 
needs of the service area. Based on the review of information provided, the System appears to be 
functioning satisfactorily. Portions of the System are past their useful life and therefore pose additional 
risk for potential sewer line failures. The WPC capital plan has been developed with a focus on 
minimizing any such risks. 
Available data indicates that WPC is operating and maintaining the System in general compliance with its 
permit requirements with NEORSD and the conditions of its MS4 stormwater permit. Based on GPD’s 
observations and discussions with WPC staff, operation and maintenance of the System continues to be 
enhanced through implementation of technology and improved information management systems, 
increases in staff for operations, and increased capital investment. 
 
 gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 07242024.docx 5-9 
 
 
With respect to the pump stations, WPC’s pump stations appear to be reliable and have sufficient 
capacity. However, due to high levels of I/I the Incinerator and Edgewater Stations have highly increased 
run times during peak wet weather events. Several pump stations present potential risk and require 
investment. However, all of the pump stations are relatively small and only run a few hours per day. 
While it is difficult to assess the condition of the collection system, WPC understands the risks of its older 
system. WPC has performed a SSES project, and continues to investigate its condition and develop cost 
effective renewal and replacement plans. gpdgroup.com 
https://gpdco.sharepoint.com/sites/WPCBondsFeasibilityStudy/Shared Documents/General/Cleveland WPC Bond Feasibility Report vFINAL 
07242024.docx 
6-1 
 
 
6 CAPITAL IMPROVEMENT PROGRAM 
GPD was provided with the 2024-2028 Capital Improvement Plan, which covers the fiscal years 2024 
through 2028. This plan is provided as Appendix B and summarized below. The fiscal year 2023 capital 
plan was also provided for reference. 
 
6.1 Past Capital Improvements 
WPC historically employed a pay-as-you-go capital improvement plan, supplemented with grants and low 
cost loans from the Ohio Public Works Commission (OPWC) and Ohio Water Development Authority 
(OWDA) for eligible projects. Since 2018, WPC pursued multiple grant and funding sources as applicable 
to supplement the budgeted capital improvement plan funds. Such sources include Ohio EPA’s Water 
Pollution Control Loan Fund (WPCLF or OWDA WPCLF), NEORSD Member Community Infrastructure 
Program (MCIP) grants, NEORSD CCS funds, NEORSD Green Infrastructure (GI) grants, revenue funds 
and other applicable federal and state funds. Since the inception of the NEORSD MCIP funds, WPC has 
been awarded over $7.1 million for sewer improvement projects. Since the inception of the NEORSD 
CCS, MCIP and GI funds, WPC has been granted over $19.1 million towards projects and equipment 
used to improve and/or maintain the System. Past improvement efforts focused primarily on addressing 
both street and basement flooding, acquiring equipment needed to maintain the System and 
implementing information technology systems to better manage its assets. For example, WPC 
implemented Cityworks as its CMMS and integrated the City’s GIS to view storm and wastewater assets 
and analyze preventive maintenance issues without being on site. WPC prioritized its capital 
improvement program based on known problem areas, results of sewer investigations, customer surveys, 
service calls and complaints and data available from its information management systems.  Past CIPs 
included construction of relief main sewers, replacement of collapsed and deteriorated sewers, drainage 
improvements to reduce the risk of flooding and lining of storm and sanitary sewers. Table 6-1 presents a 
summary of the historical annual level of capital expenditure for the System.
